                                      BRAZIL
       SECOND PROGRAMMATICLOANFOR SUSTAINABLEAND EQUITABLEGROWTH
                          LOANAND PROGRAMSUMMARY



Borrower:        Federative Repub1ic o f Brazi1

Implementing     MinistryofFinance
Agency:

PovertyCategory: Not Applicable

Amount:          US$601310,000

Terms:           Fixed-spread loan in U.S. dollars, with level repayments of principal
                 starting in 2001 and ending in 2022, and payment dates o f Jun 15 and
                 December 15.

CommitmentFee:   Eighty five one-hundredths o f one per cent (0.85%) per annum from the
                 date on which the Commitment Charge commences to accrue in
                 accordance with the provisions o f Section 3.01 o f the General Conditions
                 to but not including the fourth anniversary o f such date; and (ii)seventy
                 five one-hundredths o f one per cent (0.75%) per annum thereafter; subject
                 in either case to any waiver o f a portion of such charge as may be
                 determined by the Bank from time to time.

Front-EndFee     US$ 1,503,775

Objective:       The main objective o fthis program is to support sustainable and equitable
                 economic growth in Brazil. The program will raise Brazil's sustainable
                 economic growth potential, increase employment and reduce poverty. In
                 logistics, key actions will cut customs release times by 40 percent, cut
                 container handling costs in ports by 10percent, lower road transport costs
                 by about 5 percent and increase non-road transportation by 10 percent.
                 Improvements to the business environment will increase public-private
                 partnership, increase cartel prosecutions by the competition authorities,
                 halve the time to register a business in selected cities, and increase the
                 speed o f resolution and the recovery value o f insolvent enterprises under
                 the new bankruptcy law. Financial sector reforms will reduce bank
                 overheads, increase financial access and reduce credit risk, accelerate the
                 expansion o f the insurance industry, and increase access to bank accounts
                 from 95 million to 103 million people by the end o f the program. In
                 innovation, a new innovation law will increase technology transfer
                 contracts between universities and the private sector by 20 percent and
                 increase the private share in R&D by 10 percentage points. Supported
                 improvements in the Clean Development Mechanism will generate at
                 least US$lOO million in carbon credits.




                                      vii

Description:   The proposed loan supports a second set o f microeconomic and
               institutional reforms (the Growth Program) that foster sustainable and
               equitable growth by favoring capital accumulation and productivity gains
               in Brazil. It seeks to deepen the Program initiated by the First Loan by
               consolidating key progress in the originally four selected areas -- (i)
               reducing logistics costs, (ii) enhancing business environment, (iii)
               improving financial sector efficiency, access, and soundness, and (iv)
               increasing technological progress and innovation - and defining new
               conditions for further continuation.

Benefits       A successful program of continued sound economic management,
               structural reforms, and implementation o f actions in the four areas
               supported by the loan could contribute to Brazil reaching an average
               annual growth rate o f 4 percent or more over the next five years. The loan
               deepens a broad, ambitious, coherent, and politically feasible set o f
               microeconomic reforms for sustainable, broad-based growth, employment
               generation, and poverty reduction through higher investment and rising
               productivity. It strengthens a long-term strategic framework for reforms,
               enhancing financial flows tied to the reform program and providing
               technical assistance where international experience can improve policy or
               institutional design. Increasing growth equitably i s important to provide
               employment and raise the incomes o f the poor, directly reducing poverty.
               Higher growth is also important to reinforce Brazil's economic stability
               by reducing debt ratios and to finance Brazil's new and effective social
               programs. Actions to increase productivity are important to make growth
               sustainable:   environmentally,    through lower resource usage, and
               economically, by increasing export competitiveness and thus reducing
               reliance on foreign savings. Measures to reinforce carbon trading are
               expected to improve environmental sustainability. The loan will also
               reduce external vulnerability by covering part o f Brazil's external
               financing requirements and bolstering net international reserves.

R i s k s      (i) External vulnerability: a downturn in emerging market credit
               conditions could harm growth prospects. Mitigation: Brazil i s in a much
               better position to deal with external shocks. Since the previous operation,
               Brazil's risk premium dropped more than 200 points (to less than 400),
               reflecting an overall improvement in the country's macroeconomic
               performance, including a trade volume o f 31 percent o f GDP;
               international reserves up to US$ 60 billion, a net debt to GDP ratio down
               by 7 percentage points to 50 percent with a primary surplus o f 5.2 percent
               by late 2005.
               (ii) Fiscal risks: general elections in 2006 may decrease fiscal discipline
               thereby undermining prospects for sustainable growth, as emphasis on
               short term results may be detrimental to measures with a medium term
               impact. Mitigation: the programmatic framework means that future loans
               depend on the integrity o f both growth and macroeconomic policy.
               Government has demonstrated a high degree o f commitment with its

                                         ...
                                       Vlll

                 current fiscal targets. Risks are also mitigated by the complementary
                 Fiscal Reform Loan series and Technical Assistance (TA).
                 (iii) Continuity o f the Program: general elections in 2006 may change
                 Brazil's growth strategy and therefore interrupt the progress o f reforms in
                 the supported areas. Mitigation: Brazil had established a good precedent
                 interms of economic policy transition inthe politically sensitive fields of
                 monetary and fiscal policies. Areas supported by the loan tend to be
                 politically neutral with a positive social impact. Support to the Program
                 has increased over the years.
                 (iv) Risks that the set o f actions covered by the operation is not defined
                 broadly enough: e.g., such risks come from the energy sector regulatory
                 framework and other areas o f the economy such as trade and labor
                 regulations. Mitigation: Recent increase in private investment, labor
                 productivity and formal employment are favorable evidence to the set o f
                 selected areas. Areas not covered (such as energy) have been gradually
                 settled or included in the Program (such as contract enforcement).
                 Discussions on further trade liberalization were also initiated, showing
                 interest with the enlargement o f the reforms.

FinancingPlan    Single tranche disbursed after effectiveness.

Net PresentValue Not Applicable

ProjectIDNumber PO95675

                                                  EXECUTIVE        SUMMARY

1.        This document proposes a Single-Tranche Second Programmatic Loan for
Sustainable and Equitable Economic Growth to the Federative Republic of Brazil in the
amount of US$601,510,000.The proposed loan would support a critical mass o f microeconomic
and institutional reforms in the four areas originally selected by the First Programmatic Loan for
Sustainable and Equitable Growth (FPLSEG).

2.        The FPLSEG (January 2004) supported a program of reforms that constituted the core
of a new sustainable and equitable growth strategy (The Growth Program) for Brazil. It
targeted investment and productivity while avoiding inefficient microeconomic policies or the
creation o f fiscal liabilities. Microeconomic, institutional and policy reforms were started in the
areas o f (i)logistics, (ii)        business environment, (iii)financial efficiency and depth, and (iv)
innovation and technology policy. The Government was also acting on a longer list o f growth-
oriented reforms that included improving contract enforcement and strengthening the labor
market, parts of which could be incorporated into this program later on.'

3.        World Bank support was subsequently extended to institutional and microeconomic
reforms in the housing sector by another development policy loan (the Housing Loan, June
2005).2 Brazil's aim at creating an efficient unified formal housing market based on private
capital i s a clear departure from a history o f directed credit and market segmentation. The
housing sector reform program would deepen one o f the four reform areas o f the FPLSEG by
strengthening housing finance and, in the medium-term, contributing to reinforce the equity
pillar o f the growth program. As stated in the program document for that operation, extension o f
Bank support to the housing sector was made possible due to solid progress achieved by Brazil in
the four reform areas selected by the first operation inthe growth cluster.

4.        The proposed second operation would consolidateprogress achieved by the Brazilian
Government during 2004-2005 in thefour areas originally selected by thefirst loan. The pillar
of reforms adopted since the first operation i s the approval by Congress inDecember 2004 o f the
Public-Private Partnership (PPP) Law, the Bankruptcy and Insolvency Law and corresponding
amendments to the National Tax Code.3 Additional steps, although unevenly distributed among
the four areas, indicate a reasonable development level of the overall program and provide the
"critical mass" required for the proposed operation. Changes expected in a later phase o f the
program, inparticular the enactment o f the Innovation Law, were also a~hieved.~Reforms inone
"big ticket item" -namely thejudiciary reform-were initiated with an important Constitutional
Amendment (see Annex 8).

5 .       Conditions had been describedfor the second and third loans and long-term goals have
been identified in allfour areas, with an implied path of reforms over time. Reform progress
was not expected to occur at uniform speed across components; indeed this would be unrealistic,

' SeeWorld Bank Report No. 27507-BR. "First Programmatic Loan for Sustainableand EquitableGrowth.". ProgramDocument.January 2004.

                                                                  1

given their depth and scope. Thus, the Bank would support subsets o f the reform agenda as the
Government progresses in one or more areas. The next (third) loan would be enabled when the
key next steps (triggers) in one or several program areas have been met and, at the same time, a
critical mass o f reform measures from the broader matrix have been achieved, such that there
will be sufficient confidence in substantial progress towards the development outcomes in terms
o f equitable and sustainable growth.

6.         Progress in the implementation of the Growth Program during 2004-2005 significantly
surpassed the thresholds defined in either case discussed by the FPLSEG. The case o f
"Uneven Reform Progress across Pillars" expected that the second loan would be primarily
based on progress in the business environment area, most notably the approval o f PPP and
Bankruptcy and Insolvency Laws, with some critical mass coming from other pillars.
Alternatively, the case o f "More Uniform but Slower Progress," in the business environment
field would be compensated by faster pro ess in the logistics and innovation fields, possibly
with key measures coming in new areas. Despite uneven progress, accomplishments of the
                                                        gr
Growth Program in 2004-2005 clearly include key ingredients o fboth cases.

7.         The proposed loan is grounded in the satisfactory performance achieved by the
FPLSEG, as discussed in a recent Bank reporL6 The Government's program was considered
ambitious, but technically sound, internally consistent, and politically feasible. Its sequencing
and timing were propitious, addressing difficult long-term structural and institutional issues
during the returnto economic expansion. In addition, discussions on broader reforms - such as
contract enforcement, pension, tax, and trade policy - were initiated and some preliminary
initiatives were undertaken. Efficient macroeconomic management was strengthened as an
essential component o f the overall development strategy.

8.         The Growth Program should not be seen, however, as a substitute for continued
structural reforms, including the tax reform, and betterpublic expenditures. Indeed, there i s a
nested relationship among the development strategy, the broader microeconomic growth agenda,
and the growth program - as supported by the FPLSEG (See Figure 3.1). The development
strategy includes macroeconomic stability and targeted social policies. Two separate streams of
development policy loans are supporting progress o f the fiscal reform, to improve the quality of
fiscal adjustment, as well as o f education and healthcare policy reforms, The broader
microeconomic agenda clearly includes elements that were not supported by the FPLSEG, but
could be brought within its follow-up operations, such as tax reform, trade policy, and labor
reform. Initiatives to improve contract enforcement are supported by the current loan.

9,         Indeed, the relationship between economic growth, economic performance, and the
dynamics of debt should be underlined as a key part of Brazil's development strategy. Inrecent
years, the Government o f Brazil increased primary fiscal surplus and arrested the upward trend
inthe public debt burden. Discipline resulted ina surplus inthe current account ofthe balance o f
payments, a rare event with no precedent inthe previous three decades. It has already resulted in
clear gains. InOctober 2003, the interest premium paid by Brazil's sovereign debt was 600 basis

programmaticLoanfor Sustainableand EquitableGrowth.ReportN.32604-BR.



                                                               2

points over the U S treasury bills; currently, it i s 250 points, which, by itself, implies savings o f
about 2 percent of GDP in debt service - resources that can now be used for investment and
public service delivery.

10.     Recent developments in thefield of trade policy also indicate progress of the broader
agenda. Neither unilateral trade liberalizationnor aggressive concessions in exchange for market
access are consensual strategies among Brazilian policy-makers. Consensus about the
importance o f international trade for development i s gradually evolving in Brazil. This, however,
should not obscure the impressive progress in trade integration made so far: in the last 5 years,
trade as a share o f GDP increased from 22 to 34 percent. This evolution represents an
improvement over the 1990s' results (between 20 and 22 percent) and still with a steep
ascending trend. It i s also worth noting the result-oriented approach to international negotiations,
such as the one adopted duringthe Hong-Kong meeting o f the WTO's Doha Round.

11.     Acknowledgement of satisfactory implementation of the Growth Program should not
be understood as an implicit endorsement of all aspects of the investment climate in Brazil.
Taxation remains among the highest and most cumbersome in the world. The time usually
required to open a new business favors informality; labor regulations do not encourage long-term
employment relations; contract enforcement i s too costly; and, the regulatory risk hampers
private investment in infrastructure. Moreover, public savings are excessively low; financial
needs o f the public sector continue to crowd-out private investors; and publicly owned-banks
still play a large role in credit markets. In large and complex democratic nations, such as Brazil,
progress will come whenever and wherever possible: the Bank should neither step inthe way nor
attempt to force order.

12.     I n addition, the growth performance of the Brazilian economy during 2004-2005 was
rather modest. The growth rate in 2005 declined to merely 2.5 percent after a 4.9 percent rise in
2004. Most observers associate this contraction in economic activity with exogenous factors
affecting the economy inthe third quarter o f 2005, such as the political turmoil and the decreased
demand for agricultural machines decrease due to the occurrence o f droughts in selected areas. It
is unlikely, however, that these variables have affected the long-term growth prospects o f the
Brazilian economy.

13.     But, not surprisingly, projections for 2006 are more optimistic. This optimism i s
partially explained by improvements in core determinants o f long-term growth in Brazil. First,
the investment ratio, around 20 percent o f GDP in 2005, is behind the Brazilian needs but
indicates the consolidation o f a recovery initiated in early 2004. Also worth noting i s the
increasing participation o f machinery and equipment instead o f civil construction, which may
yield higher growth impacts. Labor productivity i s also increasing and, for the first time in a long
period, i s combined with the rise of formal employment. With national savings at 23 percent of
GDP and a comfortable external situation, the historically important savings constraint seems not
to be binding.

14.     The proposed loan would help to consolidate the Brazilian Growth Program and
strengthen its implementation in a critical moment. Since coming to power, the present
administration has maintained an excellent track record o f policy-setting, showing strong
commitment in the face of strong odds to combine macroeconomic rigor with social objectives.



                                                   3

Implementing microeconomic and institutional reforms, however, i s a process - not an event.
Lower than expected results inthe short-term should not be seen as a reason for change.

15.         The Government has requested the continuation of Bank support. The proposed loan
would form part o f a programmatic framework, entailing financial support for prior progress
along with continued support to a multifaceted microeconomic reform program. The program
allows the necessary flexibility to respond to unpredictable economic realities and political
constraints across a broad front o freforms.

16.         Brazil achieved important outcomes in the implementation of its strategy to promote
substantial reduction of logistic costs. As a result o f the implementation o f part o f the customs
reform strategy supported by the FPLSEG, selectivity levels were reduced from 40 to 30 percent
and the average release time o f merchandise decreased by 20 p e r ~ e n t .A~ new regulatory
environment for Portos Secos will facilitate entry by the private sector and expedite even further
the process o f customs administration. In the roads sector, the use o f output-based maintenance
and rehabilitation contracts increased to roughly 22 percent o f total, short o f the program target
(30 percent). An extension o f 6,878 km o f non-trunk roads under federal responsibility were
transferred to the states during 2004-2005, more than the established target (12 percent), In
addition, maintenance and rehabilitation expenditures received high priority by the Pilot Project
in Infrastructure (PPI), an additional sign of commitment of the current administration with the
reduction o f logistics costs.

17.         The business environment has improved through the approval of the PPP and the
Bankruptcy and Insolvency Laws.                            The approval by Congress o f the PPP Law (Law No.
11,079/2004) and the further development o f complementary legislation are key steps for the
efficient      use o f public resources in infrastructure. Measures to strengthen the regulatory
environment were also taken with the approval o f a law creating a career development plan for
the staff o fregulatory agencies (Law No. 10,871/2004) and the submission to Congress o f a draft
bill regulating the water and sanitation sectors. The final approval of the Bankruptcy and
Insolvency Law (Law No. 11,101/2005) i s o f pivotal relevance, not only because the legal
reform inthis field was delayed for more than ten years in Congress but also because its impact
on improving firms' turnaround and preserving intangible assets i s expected to be high.' A
proposal to reform the antitrust system was also sent to Congress, after more than five years o f
discussions within the Executive.

18.         Legal reforms to improve contract enforcement were submitted to Congress, a result
that comes unexpectedly early in the reform process. The Government sponsored several Bills
of Law to improve procedural issues and support alternative dispute resolution mechanisms.'
These are part o f a broader and more ambitious strategy for the judiciary reform that aims,

among other things, at reducing inefficiencies incontract enforcement coming from court delays.
Further analysis o f the judiciary sector and its needed reform was presented in a Bank report in
2003." The FPLSEG did not directly support reforms in this field but acknowledged that this
would be a desirable way to expand the Program, given its importance for increasing economic
growth inthe next few years."

19.       The reduction of the cost of doing business acquiredprominence in the reform agenda
with results likely to appear in the near future. Currently there are Facil Centers - a joint
initiative by Brazilian Service to Support Micro and Small Companies (SEBRAE) and National
Department o f Commerce Registry (DNRC) to deliver integrated services for company
registration - in 11 o f the 21 capital cities and, in most o f them, the time spent to open a firm is
less than 10 days. InDecember 2004, an agenda for actions -encompassing (i)a better provision
o f information; (ii)improvement mechanisms o f previous consultation; and, (iii)the
establishment o f a single point o f entry for data and documents by the creation o f an electronic
single registry form - was agreed upon by the institutions involved. A general law on micro and
small enterprises prepared by SEBRAE that proposes the simplification o f tax incidence and
collection on micro, small and medium enterprises was sent to Congress. In January 2006, the
Government sent to Congress a draft law that simplifies firm registry - expected to reduce from
152 to 15 days the time requiredto open a new business inBrazil.

20.      A draft law modernizing regulation in the re-insurance market was submitted to
Congress while initiatives tofacilitate credit access and reduce spreads were undertaken. The
main goal is to end the legal monopoly o f Instituto de Resseguros do Brazil (IRB) and thereby
introduce competition in the re-insurance services industry. By simply granting access o f
consumers to foreign providers, the Government managed to bring premium charged by IRB
down by 30 percent in2005. Another objective of the draft law i s to transfer regulatory functions
to sector specific regulators - the Superintendtncia de Seguros Privados (Susep) and the
Conselho Nacional de Seguro Privado (CNSP). Other actions in the financial sector included the
creation o f the payroll deduction loan and new securitizationmechanisms for the agricultural and
real estate sectors, initiatives that improved access to credit and reduced spreads. The amount o f
credit provided through payroll deduction loans, for example, doubled during 2004, reaching
approximately RS$ 12 billion and increased to RS$ 17.8 billion in May 2005, while its monthly
cost has been, on average, 2 percentage points lower than the traditional personal loans.

21.       Significant steps are being taken in Brazil to restructure the portfolio and modernize
the management of Government-owned banks. The asset quality o f Caixa Econ5mica Federal
has recently improved as its balance sheet has been cleaned o f many subsidized loans. Banco do
Brasil has achieved a modernization o f risk management policies and needs to continue reducing
non-performance loans (NPLs) on new loans and expand private participation in its equity.
BNDES has been among the most successful federal banks, but it has now to adjust to higher
cost funding and avoid crowding out private market development. Recent reforms appear to go
inthe right direction. However, more progress is necessary. Most importantly, there is a need to
involve public banks more directly in the reform o f the financial infrastructure (including credit

standardize contracts inprimary and especially secondary securities markets.

22.      The Innovation Law, an essential tool to improve the productive use of scientific
knowledge and the efficiency of public R&D, was approved in December 2004. The law (Law
No. 10,973/2004) better distributes property rights over inventions generated by publicly-funded
research, allowing public researchers to financially benefit from their own inventions. It thereby
creates a legal environment favorable to results-oriented research and to technology transfer from
the public to the private sector. The law also provides for the direct use o f public funds to finance
private research (subvenqiio econdmica), paving the way for the reduction o f cumbersome and
ineffective triangulations within the private sector and public research centers, which still
predominate nowadays. By the end o f the program, this initiative may help to increase the share
of private R&D intotal R&D expenditures by up to 10 percentage points.

23.     Subject to reform progress, a third loan is envisaged as part of the original program.
The operation will be enabled when substantial progress toward the implementation o f the
overall program i s achieved (i.e., reducing logistics costs, improving the business environment,
enhancing and deepening the financial sector, and increasing innovation capacity), focusing
particularly on the key next steps summarized inthe third column o f the Bank Matrix (Annex 2).
Substantial progress, therefore, will be indicatedby the accomplishment o f key next steps and an
advancement of complementary measures (critical mass) that will contribute to the achievement
o f outcomes and goals established by the program. Substantial progress might be achieved, for
instance, in terms o f improving the business environment and enhancing financial depth and
efficiency. In the field o f business environment, key next steps are the consolidation o f the
Conselho Nacional de Justiqa and the enactment o f P L 90/2005 (Sumula Vinculante). In the
financial sector, key next steps are the approval by Congress o f the insurance sector reform and
the implementation o f positive information credit reporting (CadastroPositivo).

24.      Theproposed loan has its underpinnings in analytical work done since the FPLSEG. It
i s based on extensive recent analytical work, including an Investment Climate Assessment; a
report on the cost o f doing business; a report on the costs o f contract enforcement; and, an
analysis o f the regulatory environment for infrastructure investments. It i s also complemented by
the fiscal reform series of policy-based loans, which will continue to reinforce economic
stability, and the human development series, which inter alia supports growth-enhancing
education reforms.

25.      The program is also accompanied by a Technical Assistance (TA) Loan, which is
performing reasonably well. The loan was signed in October 2005 but several activities have
been implemented through retroactive finance since mid-2004. Some o f the studies have already
generated important inputs to the development o f the project, as in the case o f studies on the time
and cost o f the judicial enforcement o f contracts that helped to frame some o f the initiatives
undertaken on this topic. By supporting ministries involved inthe four pillars o f the program, TA
activities have been important to galvanize interest and generate momentum for the progress of
sector specific reforms.

26.     Theproposed loan is clearly grounded on the CAS 2004-2007 and consistent with the
CAS mid-term review. The productivity-growth nexus is one o f the CAS pillars (see Figure 4.1).



                                                 6

The mid-term review o f the CAS evaluated achievements in this field as high. Other
programmatic series support progress in the complementary areas. The most recent operation
under the fiscal series supports a pension reform. Advancements in the enforcement o f
environmental policy were supported by a programmatic environmental loan. Progress in the
fields o f education, health and social protection policies would be supported by the upcoming
Second HD Programmatic loan.

27.     The proposed project complements analytical and operational work done by other
members of the World Bank Group. As part o f its Municipal Scorecard pilot project in the
Latin American region, IFC will measure municipal-level regulations for 25 o f Brazil's most
important cities - including SBo Paulo, Rio de Janeiro, Salvador, Brasilia, and Fortaleza. With
the Municipality o f SZo Paulo, IFC i s also supporting the reform o f the city's business
registration process. Inaddition, FIAS has taken the lead on similar work at the state-level.

28.     Brazil has used the programmatic framework successfully in the recent past. These
include the Fiscal Reform Programmatic Structural Adjustment Loan series; the human
development series and the environmental program. Inthe growth series, while exercising some
flexibility to extend the areas supported towards the housing sector with the second operation,
the proposed loan consolidates the vigorous progress achieved in the originally selected four
areas o f the program while adding two new important refinements to the reforms inthe business
environment (contract enforcement) and technology and innovation (labor training).

  INTERNATIONAL BANK FOR RECONSTRUCTIONAND DEVELOPMENT
                                   PROGRAMDOCUMENT
       FOR A SECOND PROGRAMMATIC LOANFOR SUSTAINABLEAND
                                    EQUITABLE GROWTH
                    TO THE FEDERATIVE REPUBLIC OF BRAZIL


                                        1.INTRODUCTION

1.1      With deep macroeconomic reforms but low and erratic growth, Brazil hasproved that
stabilization on its own is not enough. In the past decade, Brazil has economically transformed
itself and laid the institutional foundations for solid macroeconomic management. Chronic
inflation has been eradicated and the trio o f adept inflation targeting, fiscal balance, and a
floating exchange rate regime has created a stable macroeconomic base. Efforts have begun to
make public spending more effective in attacking poverty. Health and education indicators have
been altered using per-capita transfers to states and municipalities. Programs such as the rural
pension and conditional cash transfers (e.g., Bolsa Familia) show a heightened preoccupation
with making sure that resources reach society's poorest.

1.2      Economic growth was the missing ingredient to rendering resilient and enduring
social progress. By the 1990s, many Latin American economies hadbounced back from the "lost
decade" o f the 1980sbut for Brazil still undergoing stabilization, the 1990srepresented a second
"lost decade" for growth. While Latin American per-capita income grew at an average rate of
about 2 percent per year, slightly above the world rate, Brazil managed only about half o f that
(See Figure 1.1). In the absence of strong financial flows to emerging markets, and with the
market uncertainty o f 2002, Brazil's growth has remained disappointing, and national
unemployment has reached record levels o f around 13 percent.

                                                Figure 1.1
                  Per Capita Growth inBrazil, Latin America, and the World (inPercentage)




                       Source: World Bank (2002).




                                                     8

1.3      Brazil's new administration was elected on a platform that emphasized economic
growth and inclusion. Within Latin America, President da Silva's administration represents
perhaps the first experiment in which a democratically chosen candidate, with the strong support
of social movements in a highly unequal society, proposes to combine a clear commitment to
social justice with a sound economic program. Throughout the region, only partial combinations
of these components have so far been seen with limited success. In Latin America and beyond,
Brazil has raised expectations.

1.4      The Government provided clear signs of commitment to its strategy, Initially faced
with low investor confidence, currency depreciation, and inflation, the Government increased
interest rates and cut spending. Results have been impressive. Inflation and inflationary
expectations had been brought in line with Central Bank targets. A reform o f the public-sector
social security system was initiated. The Government has also initiated ambitious measures to
improve the impact o f social spending. A rationalization o f Brazil's system o f social assistance
transfers had been launched (Bolsa Familia) At the same time, creative mechanisms to expand
transparency and public participation in policy-making, such as the Economic Development
Council (Conselho de Desenvolvimento Econbmico), have been introduced to encourage
consensus and political cohesion around key public policies.

1.5      The 1990's performance had heightened concern - and strengthened agreement -
over Brazil's growth agenda. While continued fiscal balance i s the key to reducing real interest
rates in the long run, in the presence o f many spending rigidities this has cut into public
investment, which has fallen to about 2.6 percent o f GDP. Private investment has remained
weak, partly because it awaits a more certain investment climate, particularly in relation to
Government regulation. At the same time, there remains a series o f microeconomic impediments
to investment, innovation, and productivity, the removal o f which would provide an opportunity
to galvanize growth. The microeconomic agenda, then, provides the chance to reinforce the
macroeconomic and social reforms that Brazil has built and defended so carefully inrecent years

1.6      I n part, weak growth stems from the incompleteness of the " j h t generation"
structural reforms. Fiscal rigidities and high Government consumption leave little room for
public investments, especially in infrastructure. Government deficits continue to crowd private-
sector borrowing out o f credit markets. The financial sector i s still highly regulated and
dominated by large public sector institutions.

1.7      Yet, growth rates may be increased without immediate progress on these fronts.
Private investment may be mobilized in many sectors through improvements o f the business
environment. The Government's firm fiscal management i s bringing down borrowing costs,
creating more room for private lending.

1.8      Furthermore, to construct a sound growth strategy, Brazil needs to pursue reforms in
a sequence that makes political and economic sense. International experience has shown that,
theoretically, good reform programs may fail due to poor sequencing. In Brazil certain reforms
(e.g., hrther social security reform and drastically reducingdirected credit) could face resistance
as to be unlikely in the short run. Some reforms require institutional changes that may have been
underemphasized. Others take time to deliver their ultimate results. Speed and sequencing
matter.



                                                 9

1.9       I n January 2004, the FPLSEG supported a program of reforms that constituted the
core of a new sustainable and equitable growth strategy (The Growth Program) of the
Brazilian administration. These constituted a sub-set o f a larger set o f microeconomic measures
and institutional reforms (The Growth Agenda). The FPLSEG targeted investment and
productivity while avoiding inefficient microeconomic policies or the creation o f fiscal
liabilities. Microeconomic, institutional and policy reforms were started in the areas o f (i)
logistics, (ii)  business environment, (iii)           financial efficiency and depth, and (iv) innovation and
technology policy. The Government was also acting on a longer list o f growth-oriented reforms
that included improving contract enforcement and strengthening the labor market, parts o f which
could be incorporated into this program later on.l2

1.10       The Bank support was subsequently extended to institutional and microeconomic
reforms in the housing sector by another developmentpolicy loan (the Housing Loan, June
2005).13 Brazil's aim at creating an efficient unified formal housing market based on private
capital i s a clear departure from a history o f directed credit and market segmentation. The
housing sector reform program would deepen one o f the four reform areas o f the FPLSEG by
strengthening housing finance and, in the medium-term, contributing to reinforce the equity
pillar o f the growth program. As stated in the program document for that operation, extension o f
Bank support to the housing sector was made possible due to solid progress achieved by Brazil in
the four reform areas selected by the first operation inthe growth cluster.

1.11       The moment is right to consolidateprogress on the Growth Program. Macroeconomic
conditions have improved even further since the FPLSEG. The vast majority o f key conditions
established for a second operation were concluded, including the approval o f important
legislative pieces (key next steps) and the development o f additional complementary measures
("critical mass''). Results expected for a later phase o f program implementation were also
anticipated. Finally a new area o f reform - improving contract enforcement by the judiciary -
was also established. As discussed by a recent Bank report, these results indicate that the overall
implementation o f the Growth Program by the Brazilian Government has been satisfactory.l4

                                   2. RECENT ECONOMIC DEVELOPMENTS

2.1      Since thefirst loan, macroeconomicfundamentals of the Brazilian economy improved
even further. The austere macro-fiscal policy was, and continues to be, the main factor
responsible for the impressive improvement of market sentiment with regard to the sustainability
of the Brazilian economy and the favorable growth prospects. The Government has used the
primary surplus as a mechanism device to assure Brazil's credibility in financial markets and to
guarantee the sustainability o f public debt. The current administration has repeatedly
demonstrated its commitment to fiscal discipline by announcing and achieving high and
increasing primary surpluses which grew fiom 3.9 percent o f GDP in2002 to 4.9 percent of GDP
in2005.

2.2      The responsiblefiscal stance resulted in significant fall of public debt as share of
GDP. The aftermath o f the 2002 crisis and the high interest rates used to reduce inflationary
pressures in the first semester o f 2003 and the GDP stagnation in2003 resulted inhigher debt to
GDP ratio. However, the increase of the primary surplus, the strong GDP growth and the lower
interest rates led debt to fall from 57 percent o f GDP to 51 percent between January and
December of 2004. Again, despite the large primary surplus that the Government generated in
2005, the slower GDP growth and the declining interest rates has attenuated the decline in net
public debt to GDP ratio (See Figure 2.1). For 2006, further interest rate cuts and better prospects
for growth should lead to hrther declines inthe debt to GDP ratio.

2.3      Thepublic authorities have taken advantage of the improved macroeconomic scenario
to make substantial progress in the debt composition. Efforts made by public authorities to
reduce the volume o f indexed debt have been particularly significant. Exchange indexed debt and
the related Central Bank o f Brazil (BCB) issues foreign exchange swaps have fallen from a peak
of 41 percent o f total debt in 2002 to 2 percent as o f November 2005. Also, the increase in the
share o f fixed debt was significant. Fixed bonds share on domestic debt increased from a low o f
2 percent in2002-2003 to 24 percent inNovember 2005.




                                                        11

2.4      The Government has repeatedly shown a commitment to the inflation targeting
regime. Inflation fell from 12.5 percent in 2002 to 5.7 percent in 2005. The realignment o f
inflationary expectations to the BCB target zone heralded a substantial reduction o f the interest
rate from 26.5 percent in February 2003 to 16 percent inApril 2004. However, the resumption o f
inflationary pressures inthe third and fourth quarters o f 2004 forced the BCB to adopt a long and
strong monetary tighten. After 15 months o f monetary contraction, inflation and inflation
expectations converged to the BCB target (See Figure 2.2). The good inflationary prospects
allowed the BCB to cut interest rates from 19.7 percent in May 2005 to 17.25 percent per annum
in January 2006. The downward trend of future interest rates could result from better prospects
regarding inflation and the increased confidence in the continuity o f the Government's
responsible fiscal stance.

                                                  Figure 2.2
                  Brazil: Monthly Inflation Rate, Januaryi2002-October 12006(inPercentage)




2.5      Theperformance of the external sector was strong, leading to a reduction of external
vulnerability. The value of exports almost doubled fi-om 2002 to 2005. Exports have benefited
from high commodity prices and diversified industrial exports. As a result, Brazil has obtained
increasing trade surpluses for the last three years with trade balance risingfiom US$ 13 billion in
2002 to US$ 45 billion in 2005. The current account o f the balance o f payments reached a
surplus o f 0.8 percent o f GDP in 2003 and o f 1.9 percent o f GDP in 2004, and 1.8 percent o f
GDP in 2005. These results contrast with more than a decade o f large current account deficits.
Gross international reserves (including the IMF stand-by agreement resources) have reached
US$ 68 billion in 2005 or net international reserves o f US$ 53 billion (excluding the IMF
resources), a historic peak.

2.6      I n turn, reduced external vulnerability supported Brazil's external debt sustainability,
External debt sustainability indicators are the best inthe last 30 years. The strong export increase
led the interest payments to exports ratio to fall from the high level o f 35 percent in 1999 to less
than 15 percent in 2005 (See Figure 2.3). The reduction o f net external debt reinforced the
positive effect o f exports growth, promoting a significant decrease inthe net debt to exports ratio
from the 3.5 in 1999 to 1.4 in 2005 (See Figure 2.4). A continuation o f this decreasing trend of
both indicators could help the country to achieve the investment grade by rating agencies.

2.7       Not surprisingly, therefore, impressive results have been achieved in terms of the
reduction of sovereign spreads. Sovereign spreads fell from 2,400 basis points in October 2002
to less than 500 basis points in December 2003 to less than 400 basis points in2004, reaching its
historical minimum levels below 300 basis points in 2005. The robust trade and current account
balances promoted an appreciation of the exchange rate from RS$ 3.60 to the U S dollar to RS$
2.25 over the same period of time (See Figure2.5).

2.8       The improvement of macroeconomic conditions allowed Brazil not to renew the stand-
by arrangement with the IMF as investors' confidence increased. Moreover, the growing
strengthof Brazil's external position, especially continuing substantial trade and current account
surpluses, and strong capital inflows, which have greatly boosted reserves and reduced external
debt, allowed Brazil to make an early repayment o f its entire outstanding obligations to the IMF
inJanuary 2006.



                                                       13

2.9       Brazil began to pick up the returns of its sound economicpolicy in terms of growth,
income distribution, and poverty reduction. For the first time in three decades, the Brazilian
economy seems to be entering in a cycle o f sustained economic growth, improved income
distribution, and poverty reduction in a context o f low inflation, comfortable external situation,
and strict fiscal discipline. After the 2003 adjustment, the Brazilian economy experienced an
expansionary period and the growth rate increased from 0.5 percent in 2003 to 4.9 percent in
2004 (the highest rate in ten years). However, tight monetary and fiscal policies and the political
crisis decelerated the growth rate to around 2.5 percent in 2005. The end o f the monetary
contraction (initiated in September 2005), the fall o f unemployment, and the recovery o f income
depicted more favorable growth prospects for 2006. Forecasts for 2006 indicate a higher growth
rate o f 3 to 4 percent.

2.10      Income distribution and poverty reduction strongly improved in 2004. The recent
release o f the PNAD (National Household Survey) revealed the fall o f income distribution
inequality Gini index from 0.59 in 2002 to 0.58 in 2003 and to 0.57 in 2004. The poverty rate,
which had risen from 32.9 percent in 2002 to 34.1 percent in 2003, experienced a strong fall
dropping to 31.7 percent in 2004.Well targeted conditional cash transfer programs as well as the
recovery o f labor income among were the main reasons for the improvement o f income
distribution and poverty reduction.

2.1 1    Despite these impressive results, some concerns remain, in particular, with respect to
the quality ofthefiscal adjustment. The fiscal adjustment has been accomplished through strong
revenue increases (the tax burden has grown from 29 percent o f GDP in 1998 to 35 percent in
2004) and by curtailing public investment (investments by federal Government fell from
1.1 percent o f GDP in 1998 to 0.4 percent o f GDP in 2003 and 2004). The tax burden increase
and the public investment contraction are harming growth prospects and can accelerate the debt
dynamics. However, the permanent increase o f current expenditures and the tax burden increase
are negatively affecting the sustainability o f the current fiscal adjustment effort. The quality of
fiscal adjustment may, therefore, negatively impact economic growth and jeopardize its
continuation. Improved efficiency o f expenditures, thus, has become increasingly important in
the fiscal reform agenda.

2.12      There is still a long way to go before Brazil reaches an    " investment"grade. Besides
the low quality of the fiscal adjustment, the level o f public debt and its high costs represent a
permanent source of risk. On the external front, despite the fact that increasing trade and current
account balances have reduced the country's external financing requirements, the deceleration of
the world economic growth and the reduction o f the international liquidity could raise the
financing cost o f Brazil's debt and could interrupt the improving trend in Brazilian external
sustainability indicators. The good performance in the external sector could be threatened by a
further appreciation of the exchange rate. In turn, higher interest rates clearly make the external
debt dynamics less favorable.




                                                 14

                                         3. THE GROWTH PROGRAM


3.1      The current administration is committed to a development strategy that aims at raising
the growth potential of the Brazilian economy, creating employment, and reducing poverty.
The foundations o f this development strategy involve sound macroeconomic management,
targeted social policies, and a good state o f governance. Within this development strategy, a
Growth Agenda - consisting o f a broad set o f microeconomic and institutional measures that aim
at raising Brazil's potential output - was established as an action plan for the current
administration. The Growth Program, whose implementation i s supported by the Programmatic
Growth Loans series, consists o f a subset o f measures o f the Growth Agenda in four sectors: (i)
logistics; (ii)business environment; (iii)financial markets; and, (iv) innovation. The Growth
Program with 2005 achievements i s expected to be released inearly 2006 (see Figure 3.1).

                                               Figure3.1
          The Government's DevelopmentStrategy, the BroadGrowthAgenda, andthe GrowthProgram




                      Growth Program

                                                    c   ..,.

                      9  Business Environment
                         Financial Sector                           9 Macroeconomic
                         Innovation                                 - Institutions and




3.2       The Growth Program is conceived as a subset of microeconomic and institutional
reforms for which the expected (net) impact was considered significant. By targeting specific
intermediate outputs, these measures were expected to reduce obstacles for private investment
and productivity gains; thereby, increasing Brazil's growth potential over the long-term.
Intermediate outcomes evolve around four main objectives: (i)reducing logistics costs; (ii)
improving the business environment; (iii)     enhancing financial soundness, efficiency and depth;
and, (iv) transforming knowledge into productivity gains. Subsequently, partial targets were
defined and key measures - the key reforms to be achieved - were identified for each o f the four
program areas. The Government was also designing reforms in other fields, such as tax, contract
enforcement, trade integration, and labor markets, as part o f the growth agenda but they were not
originally included inthe Growth Program due to their own political and technical complexities.

3.3       Indeed, the areas chosen by the Growth Program are closely interconnected toprivate
investment and productivity gains. One World Bank study estimates that preventing banking


                                                   15

crises, deepening financial intermediation, reducing the Government burden on business, and
improving infrastructure could raise Brazil's growth rates by 2 to 3 percentage points.15
Importantly, these reform categories account for about half the authors' estimate o f the impact o f
all possible growth reforms for Brazil: in other words, the Growth Program, consisting o f
transport, business environment, financial, and innovation policies, represents a critical mass o f
Brazil's reform agenda for economic growth. Assuming reforms with only half o f this impact,
we estimate that the present reform program could have growth effects o f the order o f 1 percent
per annum ifpursued to completion.

3.4         The program should also be politically feasible. Political constraints imply that this
microeconomic and institutional agenda represents a sensible reform program to be sequenced
over time, Nowadays, further unilateral trade reforms and privatizations may be politically
difficult in Brazil in the face o f opposition from diverse powerful groups. Protecting fiscal
discipline should certainly take precedence and has already cost the Government some political
capital with its traditional base. Public servants have strongly opposed the social security reform.
Social tensions have been raised by unemployment and rising violent crime in urban areas and
rural land conflicts. Reforms that impose too high costs in the short run on the affected groups
(or that appear ideologically confrontational) may not represent an optimal growth strategy
today.

3.5          The Growth Program was presented in two official documents and updated in
December 2004 by the white paper "Microeconomic Reforms and Long-Term Growth," A new
version o f the Growth Program with the main developments in2005 i s under-preparation.



A.         Reducing; Logistics Costs to Raise Productivitv and Ease Trade


3.6         Over the last 15 years, global trade has doubled but Brazil had only marginally
benefited of this increase in international trade. Averaging 17 percent o f GDP inthe late 199Os,
Brazilian trade integration was poor even when compared to other continental economies, such
as China and India. Both countries also showed higher import nominal tariffs, suggesting that the
antitrade bias o f the Brazilian economy may also have came from factors other than its trade
regime. Moreover, inter-regional trade within Brazil has increased only modestly during the
same period. That naturally leads us to the issue o f logistics costs.

3.7         I n the late 1990s, overall logistics costs in Brazil were estimated at 20.percentof GDP,
almost twice the proportion in OECD countries and a third o f firms' average operational costs.
The cost to the Brazilian economy o f additional inventory holdings exceeded 4 percent o f GDP
assuming a 15 percent interest rate.I6 High logistics costs in Brazil result, to a large extent, from
the combination o f four factors: (i)                excessive delays for customs clearance; (ii) conditions    poor
of the road network; (iii)inefficient port services; and, (iv) insufficient development of
multimodal transportation systems.


l5 Loayza, P.; P. Fajnzylber;C. Calderon.(2003). "Economic Growth inLatinAmericaand the Caribbean." Washington, D.C.: The World Bank.

I hDeCastro, N. (2001) "Freight Transportation and LogisticsinBrazil: An Overview." Backgroundpaperproducedfor the World Bank.



                                                                     16

3.8         Customs procedures remained a strong component of logistics costs. Despite some
improvements in the 1990s with the introduction o f operations information systems, customs
procedures and practices remained outdated. Customs' primary focus was still on revenue
collection, instead o f trade facilitation, border protection, and transport security. Clearance time
on imports and exports (averaging 5.0 and 2.0 days, respectively) were more than twice the
international averages.

3.9          The condition of the Brazilian road network had significantly deteriorated. Brazil's
federal paved road network, totaling about 58,000 km, was built between the 1950s and the
1970s and represented assets o f nearly US$ 10 billion. It carries most o f the country's long-
distance traffic. Maintenance has been inadequate for many years, so that today the proportion o f
network in good condition has declined to about 25 percent, the lowest percentage o f the last
twenty years. This state o f affairs adds about US$500 million per year in vehicle operational
costs, principally to the private sector. Fiscal and institutional limitations reduced public
expenditures for roads, includingmaintenance and rehabilitation.

3.10        Port costs and delays were still high due to incomplete reforms. With almost 500
million tons passing annually through its ports, Brazil hadby far the largest port industryin Latin
America. The reform of the port sector was initiated with the Port Modernization Law o f 1993,
which defined a new institutional framework emphasizing competition, decentralization, and
pri~atization.'~Significant progress came only in the late 199Os, with the concession o f major
terminals to private operators, the opening o f many private terminals for public use, and the
reform o f regulatory fi-amework.18

3.11         Thefreight transport market was dominated by trucking. Over 70 percent of Brazilian
goods are transported by road, resulting in excessive transportation costs in the long distance
market segment. Brazil's transport market, which ships mainly large volumes o f low value-to-
weight commodities over long distances, would appear to favor low-cost coastal shippingor rail-
based options. But trucking clearly dominates, with an output share o f over 80 percent (See
Table 3.1). Conservative estimates suggest that avoidable logistics costs were adding more than
US$ 1.2 billion per year to the costs o f external trade and at least US$ 1.3 billion per year to the
costs o f domestic inter-regional trade in corridors with available rail services, particularly in
poorer regions.19

3.12         The Government was committed to bringing customs to international standards by
shifting its mission from revenue collection to trade facilitation and boarder protection, As
part o f this effort, a central step was streamlining existing procedures and developing new
procedures, such as advance and express release and fast-track clearance systems. These
included the expansion o f Blue Lane and the adoption o f enhanced risk management strategies
for which the Radar system was a starting point. Customs effectiveness would also require a
revision o f its human resources policy to support staff during the reform process and give the

3.13      To address the main issues in the road sector, the strategy involved four main
initiatives. First, the reclassificationo f the road network with a view to maintaining under federal
jurisdiction only the main interregional and interstate highways o f national interest (estimated at
about 43,500 km), transferring to state jurisdiction highways o f mainly local interest (about
14,500 km). Second, transferring highway sections with sufficient traffic to private
concessionaires (on up to about 10,000 km). Third, contracting out road rehabilitation and
maintenance combined on entire routes o f the remaining federal network through long-term and
output-based contracts (on about 16,000 km). Fourth, restructuring administration, terminating
DNER, and establishing a national department of transport infrastructure (DNIT) responsible for
executing public sector programs.

3.14      Completing the port reform would bring the cost of port services closer to
international levels. Inparticular, progress on three fronts i s needed: (i)         the consolidation o f the
regulatory framework;         (ii)the restructuring of port administrations; and, (iii)further
consolidation o f labor productivity. On average, the completion o f the reforms could bring cargo
transit time down by 30 percent and reduce container handling costs by 10 percent. Clear
guidelines were needed to put into practice the framework defined by the Port Modernization
Law (Law No. 8,630/1993) and the Transport Sector Restructuring Law (Law No. 10,233/2001),
especially with respect to the attributes o f different public institutions (Le., Port Councils, Port
Authorities, Dock Companies, and the federal regulatory agency). Several authorities have
inherited debt obligations beyond their financial capacities. In addition, with little competition
among ports and terminals, private operators and unions lack incentives to solve pending labor
issues.

3.15       The development of efficient nzultimodal logistics also implies aparticularly complex
agenda of measures that goes from better regulation to improved planning capaciv. The
creation o f the Inter-Ministerial Committee for Integration o f Transport Policies (CONIT) and
the reorganization o f the Transport Ministry would increase the Government's capacity to
formulate transport policies and plan the development o f the needed infrastructure. Accordingly,
rules and standards for the development o f multimodal services need to be developed. Rules are
needed to: (i)  define responsibilities o f each mode; (ii) liabilities o f operators with respect
                                                                   state



                                                      18

to individual carriers; (iii)adopt a unified bill o f landing; and, (iv) solve insurance and taxation
issues. Moreover, better regulation o f the network system as well as its geographic restructuring
inthe southeast aiming at increasing its competitiveness were also amongthe requiredinitiatives,


B.        Improvingthe Business Environment2'


InfrastructureRegulation

3.16       Investments in infrastructure as a share of GDP in Brazil declinedfrom an average of
5.0 percent during the 1980s to less than 2.0percent during the second half of the 1990s. This
performance compares poorly internationally. For example, Chile's infrastructure investments as
a share o f GDP increased from an average o f 3.5 percent to an average o f 5.5 percent inthe same
period. The contribution of infrastructure investments to the national gross fixed capital
formation (GFCF) declined from 5.4 percent o f GDP during the 1970s to roughly 2.5 percent in
2000, limiting the expansion of potential output. Brazil carried out structural reforms in most
infrastructure sectors but had disappointing results interms o f investment expansion.

3.17       Low investment rates resulted, at least in part, from the incompleteness of the legal
environment in selected sectors, the inappropriateness of concession designs; and the
weaknesses of regulatory governance. The least developed regulatory framework i s inthe water
and sanitation sector but some major progress is still required to consolidate the legal framework
in the electricity sector and increase market opportunities in the gas and railway industries.2'
Cumbersome procedures for obtaining environmental licenses are another source o f risk,
particularly in the energy and road sectors. Ill-defined property rights and liabilities in
concession contracts generate frequent conflicts that facilitate opportunism and increase risk and
some improvement in contract design was also needed.22The most urgent governance issue was
staff policy, as regulatory agencies had not been allowed to establish their own hiring
mechanisms, compensation, or career policies being staffed by temporarily assigned employees,
many originally employed by former regulatory bodies or former state-owned firms. Finally,
good governance requires the appropriate definition o f boundaries between regulators and the
executive branch.

3.18        The details of the new power sector model are gradually becoming clearer. In
December 2003, the Government announced a new model, attempting to correct some o f the

attempts have been blocked by court actions, with final Supreme Court decisions still pending. The Government is sponsoring
one Legal Bill (PL 41047/2001) to regulate this subject.

problems exist in water (the city of Limeira, SBo Paulo state), toll roads (Parana state), and electricity (Minas Gerais state).




                                                                 19

shortcomings due to the incomplete implementation o f the previous model. The main objectives
the Government identified were guaranteeing electricity supply and achieving reasonable
regulated consumer tariffs. At the same time, the Government preserved some o f the pillars o f
the old model, such as competition "for" and "in" the power market, and confirmed its intention
of abiding by all existing contracts. Execution o f the new model i s fulfilling most o f the follow-
up actions described inthe 2001 Energy Sector Reform Loan.23Nonetheless, in the eyes of the
private investor, despite all the ongoing efforts, the new power sector model is still uncertain.
Some investors fear that a concentration o f power in the hands o f the line ministry may preempt
some o f ANEEL's role as an independent regulatory agency. Brazil still has excess capacity, but
clarity o f the new model i s essential to initiate a new investment cycle. The Government,
however, managed to substantially reduce the risk o f shortage inthe near term.

3.19        High rates of renegotiation of contracts are a widespread event in the Latin American
region (LAC) but Brazil seems to show relatively higher incidence levels. Contract
renegotiations may help to address the inherently incomplete nature o f concession contracts, but
excessive renegotiations may indicate asset expropriation activity as well as create uncertainties
in the process o f competitive biddings from the private sector. In the 1990s, 41 percent o f
concession contracts in Brazil were eventually renegotiated, compared to 28 percent in LAC,
most of them were renegotiated during the initial three years aiming at reviewing tariffs. Not
surprisingly, the average return for shareholders in infrastructure inBrazil during 1996-2001 was
3 percent, compared to 6 percent in LAC, while the cost o f capital in Brazil was three percentage
points higher than inArgentina and twice as high as inChile.

3.20         Good regulatory governance is essentialfor the appropriate enforcement of contracts
and regulations. For example, beyond formal independence, regulatory agencies should justify
in a transparent and technical way the rationale for their decisions. Even the well-respected
energy regulator (ANEEL) has received criticism in this regard. Furthermore, judicial review of
regulatory decisions takes place at the first level o f the judiciary, which lacks relevant expertise
and training. Until recently, regulatory agencies had not been allowed to establish their own
hiring mechanisms, compensation, or career policies, relying basically on temporarily assigned
employees, many originally employed by former state-owned firms. Overall, agencies still lack
the skills, information, and regulatory instruments to pursue oversight functions, such as
reviewing tariff structures and monitoring compliance with standards. It will be necessary to
develop regulatory accounting and information standards, cost and financial models, and
efficiency and productivity measures.24

3.21        Reduced public sector investment is another underlying cause for low investment
levels in infrastructure. Between 1980 and 1988, public investments kept an average of
approximately 5 percent o f GDP, declining to roughly 3 percent during 1989-1993, and further
reducing to lower than 1 percent for the period 1994-1999. Two events help to explain, at least
partially, these results: (i)the 1988 Constitution's increase in mandatory social expenditures;

23 World Bank, ReportNo. P7532-BR (2002)

executive or legislature, creating "self-legislation" by infrastructure agencies (e.g., asset price evaluation in the electricity sector); (iii)the
involvement o f agencies outside their conceptual mandate (e.g., universal services, granting concessions, conducting auctions); and (iv) the lack
of clear planning by the executive body. Finally, sector laws are imprecise about accountability and tmnsparency. The Government is currently
sponsoring a law inCongress (PL 3,337/2004) to clarify some of these issues.



                                                                       20

and, (ii) privatization process that reduced the requirements for public investment. Decline in
             the
public investment has been particularly accentuated in the energy and transport sectors. Public
investment in energy declined from 2.5 percent to less than 1 percent o f GDP during 1987-1990;
increased to almost 1.5 percent during 1991-1993; and, declined again to less than 0.25 percent
of GDP duringthe rest o f the decade. Public investmentinroads has fluctuated with even greater
amplitude: from 0.15 percent o f GDP in 1986 to less than 0.05 percent in 1989, then back to 0.15
percent in 1992 and falling again to 0.05 percent in 1994 to stabilize around 0.75 percent o f
GDP, less than 1/3 the average value in the early 1980s.

3.22         I n this context, the absence of an appropriate basis for efficient public-private
partnerships in infrastructure projects was a central obstacle as well. The legal framework for
investments in infrastructure- either pure concession contracts or pure execution o f public work
-didnotallow for anefficientuseofpublicresources.Brazilmissedthe legalframework that
allowed for public-private partnerships (PPP) in infrastructure when pure concessions were not
possible and pure public service was not needed. This "middle-of-road" alternative did not find
an appropriate legal basis on either the Concession Law (Law No. 8,987/1995) or the
Procurement and Public Service Laws (Law No. 8,666/1994 and Law No. 9,074/1995,
respectively).

Facilitating Entry and Exit: The Bankruptcy and Competition Laws

3.23         Competition law enforcement is gradually evolving in Brazil. Law No. 8,894/1994
broadly resembles competition laws in other countries, legislating for anticompetitive behavior,
including the abuse o f market dominance, as well as for the control o f mergers. It also grants
formal independency to CADE (Conselho Administrativo de Defesa Econdmica), the body
rendering final decisions on antitrust cases, through fixed two-year mandates for commissioners
(extendable once). It extends to SEAE (Secretaria de Acompanhamento Econdmico) in the
Ministryof Finance economic advisory roles previously restricted to SDE (Secretariade Direito
Econdmico) in the Ministry o f Justice, the latter being responsible for carrying out preliminary
investigations and handling administrative procedures before submitting files with opinions to
CADE.25Despite this complexity, enforcement has made discernible progress, not only in terms
of the number o f cases on which CADE has ruled, but also in terms o f the advocacy roles
increasinglyplayed by SEAE and SDE.

3.24         Two remaining weaknesses were the small number of hard-core cartel cases
condemned and the complex enforcement of merger control provisions. Contrasting with the
growing emphasis on anti-cartel enforcement adopted by other antitrust agencies and with the
condemnation o f more than 15 international cartels since the mid 1990s, only one domestic cartel
case had been successfully prosecuted by CADE between 1994 and 2000. This low activity on
hard-core cartel prosecution owed partly to CADE's limited investigative powers and partly to
increases inmergers submittedfor reviews (merger reviews rose from 46 in 1997 to 226 in 1999,
584 in 2002, and 651 in 2004). Moreover, the multiplicity o f agencies involved and a low




2sClark, J. (2000) "Competition Policy and Regulatory Reform in Brazil: A Progress Report." OECD CompetitionPolicy Review, Vol. 2, No.3.;
and OECD (2001). "Brazil - OECD Economic Surveys," Pans:OECD.




                                                                   21

threshold for submission o f mergers caused delays in the enforcement o f merger control.26
Finally, mergers must be filed 15 days after "occurrence" and private sector actors disputed the
legal definition o f occurrence, since CADE has been particularly aggressive in fining non-
compliance with the 15-day rule. Moreover, post-merger notification makes it harder for CADE
to adopt structural remedies for anticompetitive mergers: most remedies have been behavioral,
which international experience has shown to be much less effective at protecting competition and
consumer welfare.

3.25        Institutional fragmentation and the lack of a stable corps of qualified professionals
are probably the most immediate obstacles for stronger competition law enforcement. The
Brazilian system i s unique in that three Government bodies are involved in competition law
enforcement, working within an unclear division o f legal attributions. Enforcement is, then,
further weakened by dispersed actions and high transaction                                                    Efficiency could be
improved by a review o f staffing assignments across the three organizations. Salaries are
uncompetitive with the private sector although the three institutions have a significant annual
budget (more than US$10million excluding salaries).

3.26        The major weakness in the framework for creditor rights and turnaround of viable
enterprises were related to its antiquated structure largely embedded in the 1945 Lei de
Fali?ncias.28Poorly designed procedures surrounding corporate insolvency hindered turnaround
of viable enterprises in distress and impeded the efficient functioning o f credit markets. As a
consequence, the cost o f exit o f non-viable companies increased while productivity and
employment inthe real economy reduced.

3.27        The availability and the cost of credit in Brazil has been significantly affected by its
weak framework for creditor rights. Difficulties associated with judicial debt collection
weakened creditors' rights and rendered credit more expensive.29Although it provides for both
concovdata (reorganization in the case o f insolvency) andfaZ6ncia (liquidation) processes, the
system has been characterized by weak creditor protection and few meaningful rehabilitation
options, resultingin disproportionately high default rates o f potentially viable companies, long
delays, and erosion o f asset value in liquidation processes.

3.28        The reorganization option (concordata) only provided for a moratorium on debt to
avoid or suspend the declaration of bankruptcy and did notpermit negotiated mechanismsfor
business reorganization. There were no provisions allowing parties (debtors/creditors) to
negotiate a plan and only unsecured creditors were encompassed. Write-offs and rescheduling of
debts were rigidly prescribed by law. There were no special regulations to ease access to credit
during the concordata process, to maintain a going concern, and the entity may not dispose of
real property or give secured guarantees without court authorization. All settlements o f debt took
