place under court jurisdiction with no voting rights for creditors. In practice, protracted court
disputes, lack o f qualified supervisors, and judicial activism in favor o f the debtor delayed
bankruptcy declaration.

3.29      Moreover, under bankruptcy and liquidation (faltncia)proceedings, the disposal of
assets was slow and ineffective. Upon declaration o f bankruptcy, pending creditor actions and
interests on debts were suspended, rendering it difficult to obtain credit to maintain a going
concern. Assets o f the estate remained unused until their sale. While the bankruptcy law allowed
the debtor to continue operating under independent management, in practice it rarely occurred.
Rights o f secured creditors are impaired by the precedence given to labor and tax claims. In
bankruptcy proceedings, secured interests rank below several other claims, including labor,
social security, and tax claims. Once bankruptcy proceedings have been concluded, there were
few funds left over even for secured creditors. In addition, assets were sold individually to the
detriment o f the borrower's intangible value.

3.30      Legal shortcomings are compounded by an insufficient regulatory and administrative
framework. The Lei de Falhzcias makes no provisions for an independent and qualified court-
appointed administrator. Judges generally appoint a lawyer to perform the role o f administrator.
There are no provisions for committees o f creditors and there i s no regulatory fi-amework for
qualifying participants, such as administrators, liquidators, and insolvency practitioners. This
leads to significant disparities in the quality and reliability o f the system. Frail state courts,
overburdened inmostjurisdictions, compoundproblems o f slowness and non-uniform quality.


AdministrativeObstacles to FirmEntryandOperation

3.3 1     The competitive environment in Brazil was also damaged by Government-generated
entry barriers. These include unduly complicated business registration processes, costly and
time-consuming labor regulations, restrictive standards registration procedures, and difficulties
in acquiring and developing land or receiving environmental licensing. These costs deterred
investment and the formalization o f new firms. Already the level o f informality, even among
formally registered firms, was substantial. Inthe recent World Bank Investment Climate Survey
o f Brazil, firms estimated that they declared only 67 to 73 percent o f their sales for tax
purposes.30Only 19 percent o f the firms in the sample (and less than half o f the large firms)
reported having audited financial statements - compared with 92 percent in Malaysia and 75
percent inChina.

3.32      Cumbersome business registration procedures and tax system are two important
obstaclesfor theformalization of SMEs in Brazil, contributing to lower productivity. Micro-
firms accounted for about one-third o f total employment in Brazil in 2000, which increased by
5.4 percent annually between 1996 and 2000 versus a 1.5 percent growth rate in large firms. In
1997-2000, birth rates o f micro-enterprises exceeded mortality rates, resulting in an annual
expansion o f 6.7 percent in the number o f micro-firms. Productivity o f large firms i s about six




  See The World Bank (2005). "Brazil: InvestmentClimate Assessment." Mimeo,




                                                                23

times higher than micro- enterprise^.^' Yet, firm growth measured by change insize classification
was in~ignificant.~~

3.33         There arefurtherpermits and licenses needed at the state and municipal levels. These
range from municipal health and fire inspections to hurdles to acquiring and developing land.
These processes show similar levels o f variation in time and cost. In many cases, the same
information or copies o f the same forms are required by multiple agencies. The cumulative effect
of these requirements can remove the incentive to formally register a business. This may lead to
a vicious circle of informality, which erodes the tax base and Government ability to provide
public goods, further reducing the attraction o f formally o erating. Firm informality deepens
failures in credit, information, and technology                            market^.^'     These factors contribute to the
phenomenon o f the "missing middle" o f the Brazilian private sector: the relative scarcity of
medium-sized firms.

3.34         Time costs are the main administrative problem for firms in Brazil. Brazil does not
have excessive pecuniary costs o f firm registry, nor does the number o f procedures to register
appear a major concern, although 15 procedures in Brazil i s still higher than Korea (12), Chile
(lo), and Mexico (7),34 But the average                      time to complete required procedures to establish a
business in Brazil (about 152 days) i s the second highest in Latin America (Figure 3.2). Brazil
ranks well behind Mexico (51 days) and takes more than five times as long as Chile (28 days).
The comparison is starker if we include some o f the more important OECD countries (Figure
10). Brazil i s significantly higher than comparators such as Mexico, Korea or Chile which
approach the OECD average o f just over 30 days. In the most innovative countries, such as
Ireland, Australia, and the US, time to register a company i s often below ten days.35

3.35         The lack of unification betweenfederal, state and municipal registration processes is
one main cause of delays. For example, a firm must register with federal, state and municipal
tax registries, which last, on average, about 25 days. By comparison, a firm can complete all
registration requirements in half that time (12 days) in Ireland. Some processes, such as the need
to be a member o f a trade association, appear to be vestiges o f the past with little value to

Contract Enforcementby the Judiciary

3.36         For some time, internal and external observers have remarked on the negative impact
of Brazil's legal andjudicial system onprivate sector development. Commentaries tend to focus
on unpredictability, delays, and high costs. In fact, Brazil's judiciary ranks fairly high as
compared to the rest of the Latin American region on other key characteristics - independence,
professionalism, or even general level o f public confidence. Brazil i s one of the first o f the
region's judiciaries to have institutionalized entrance examinations and training programs for
judges, and to maintain and use mechanisms for trackingjudicial output.

3.37         Moreover, Brazil's courts handle and decide on an unusually high caseload. Figures
for 2004 provided by the Brazil's Constitutional Court (STF) indicate that there are over 1,298
cases on average per annum and 8,621 accumulated filings per judge across the entire system
with a clearance rate (based on accumulated filings) o f 60 percent. Obviously, conditions vary
widely by region and across state and national judiciaries, but in the federal, labor, and more
advanced state courts, one can hardly accusejudges o f slacking - not when each member of the
STF, for example, is personally responsible for drafting 10,000 opinions annually or the federal
social security judges issue that manyjudgments ina few months.



  The variability of experience is quite wide. Sebrae, using expert interviews in 2000, catalogued the variation in requirements, time, and cost to
register in each state in Brazil. Of the twenty-six possible forms that a firm could be asked to produce in order to register with the state tax
authority, only one ("declaration o f individual firm") was required by all states. Mirroring this heterogeneity, the minimum time to process a
request once all forms were submitted ranges from I to 15 day and, the costs, from R$2.18 in Maranhlo to RS$ 103.5 in RondBnia (Sebrae,
2000).




                                                                       25

3.38         While Brazil has a reasonable number ofjudges and only a moderately high litigation
rate, the workload is unevenly distributed. InBrazil, there are 7.6 judges per 100,000 population
(above the recommended UN average) and 9,460 annual filings per 100,000 population.
However, congestion levels intrial courts inmore developed urban areas and insecond and third
instance courts universally, are high. Moreover, judges have not kept up with the exponential
growth in demand over the past decade, which implies that they are sitting on top o f a sizable
backlog. Unfortunately, statistics available do not provide hrther information as to what gets
decided at all, within a reasonable time, or i s retired to the backlog for years.37

3.39         Court congestion at all levels, but even more disproportionately in the courts of
appeals, is due, in substantial part, to cases in which the Government is a defendant. Most o f
these cases originate in poor service by one o f a few administrative agencies (most notoriously
the National Social Security Institute, INSS), which the plaintiff eventually wins, but only after
several years and a series o f appeals by Government lawyers (who, in fact, are under legal orders
to appeal all but the smallest awards). A recent study by the Rio de Janeiro state courts indicates
that banks and private utilities also indulge in this practice because, like the Government, they
can "control their cash flow" by delaying payments and also counting on a certain number of
legitimate claims never going                                   Courts have introduced a series o f innovations (e.g.,
completely automated processes, template judgments, and court annexed mediation) to handle
this raft o f small, repetitive cases, which also account for a good portion of the high average
case load^.^^

3.40        Regardless of the delay in reaching a first instancejudgment, a larger problem is the
high rate of appeals. For many cases, an initial judgment i s not a final resolution, because it
may well be appealed "all the way to the Supreme Court" or rather both Supreme Courts, the one
corresponding to the jurisdiction (e.g., Justice Supreme Court, STJ, for federal laws and Labor
Supreme Court, TST, for labor) and, then, the Constitutional Court (Federal Supreme Court,
STF).

3.41        Another problem was thefact that interlocutory appeals could be made separately and
would stop theforwarding process of a case until they were decided. Interlocutory appeals are
those regarding decisions prior to judgment. They were conceived to be used in situations
threatening irreversible damage to the appellant. In Brazil, however, this practice was the rule
rather than the exception as a deliberate strategy to postpone judicial decisions.

3.42         Theprocess of enforcing a court decision and collecting awards is often lengthy and
ineffectual. Enforcement can take longer than the time to judgment, if it ever produces any
results. Even here, the "judicial" obstacles are most usually procedural requirements, not
problems originating with the judge. One example is the requirement that seized assets be sold in

measuretakes an extremely sophisticated statisticalsystem, at presentonly existing ina few Brazilian states or high level courts,

the absence o f any sanction for a debtor who does not cooperate in identifying assets to be used
inlieuo fpayment.

3.43       Despite its various positive attributes, the Brazilian court system, therefore, paints a
picture of high costs and long delays in the provision of court services to citizens and
businesses, There i s a high rate o f judicial exclusion with negative effects on the economic
relations and contracts in areas such as credit and labor rights, also contributing to the highrate
of informality in the country. The quantification o f these effects i s a difficult task, but some
preliminary statistics from the credit market exemplify the adversity o f inefficient judicial
structures: an injured party that wishes to resort to Brazilian courts to enforce his or her rights
would lose between 17.0 and 43.2 percent o f the suit value (inthe highest and inthe lowest value
of the contract, respectively) by accepting a negotiated settlement, which i s the simplest route
(up to one year, provided that no embargo is filed to halt foreclosure).

3.44       More complex procedures may last up to eight years, from the stages of right
recognition, liquidation (determination of value), and execution of judgement. For a
prospective litigant, who has a right to be restored, it i s only economically viable to pursue legal
action until the end ifthe case involves very large sums or ifone employs full-timelegal counsel,
such as in the case o f major corporations. From the point o f view o f the defendant, in turn, it i s
economically advantageous to stretch the case out to the last level o f appeal. This structure of
incentives i s detrimental to the sound operation o f the economic system.


C.        Enhancing Financial Efficiency and Depth

3.45       Financial depth in Brazil, gauged by the ratio of credit to GDP, is somewhat greater
than in other major countries in the region, though considerably lower than several emerging
markets in East Asia. In nominal terms, credit has expanded, but credit as a share o f GDP has
declined, in part due to the "crowding out" of bank intermediation by Government borrowing.
(Figure 3.3) Additionally, about 40 percent of total credit inthe banking system is "earmarked"
at non-competitiverates for selected sectors, such as housing and agriculture.

3.46       Credit is not only scarce but also very expensive in Brazil, especiallyfor individuals
and small enterprises. After the hyperinflation o f the 1990s, credit spreads have decreasedto an
average of 40 percent per annum, which remains still among the highest in the world. A lot can
be explained in terms o f the macroeconomic environment (including inflation, the persistently
highSELIC rate, and the highlevel o ftaxation), while several structural reasons may also have a
role (e.g., market concentration, segmented retail payment systems, and highoperational costs).

3.47       Low intermediation and high spreads are the symptoms of a financial sector in need
of important reforms to release its significant growth potential and expand access to efficient
financial services, while preserving the stability of financial markets. Part o f these reforms i s
related to the need to continuing the enactement and enforcement o f fundamental legislation for

judgment" phase into account.




                                                                    27

  banking supervision, mobilizing long-term resources, and expanding access to credit by
  individuals and small enterprises.

  Sound Legislation,SystemRiskControlandCompetition

  3.48      A major legal impediment to the ability of Brazil'sfirtancia1sector to grow and mature
  was lifted, with the amendment of Article 192 of Brazil's Constitution. This constitutional
  provision required the financial system to be governed by a single overarching law, thus
  preventingthe development of appropriate and specialized legislation for different segments or
  themes in the financial system. Approval o f Constitutional Amendment No. 40 in 2003, paved
  the way for a series of reforms by allowing the individual treatment o f different issues by
  appropriate complementary laws. Amendment 40 also revoked ceilings o f 12 percent on interest
  rates o f non-financial entities.

  3.49      An important areafor complementary reform is credit reporting andpayment systems.
  More effective use o f positive and not only negative credit information i s needed to expand
  coverage and facilitate access to credit for underserved segments o f the population. Modernizing
  retail payment systems will also be important to build solid infrastructure for the financial sector
  expansion. Currently, inBrazil, cash and checks remain the predominant means o f payments and
  ATM networks are very fragmented. More efficient and secure payment systems are necessary to
' increase financial depth inthe country.




                                                          28

3.50        Lately, the urgent need for upgrading bank failure resolution policies has become
clear. The recent failure o f a medium-sized private bank in Brazil has proven that the existing
legal framework was still inadequate. As the financial system grows in its size and complexity,
stepping up efforts for systemic risk control has risen to the top o f the agenda o f the Central
Bank and other supervisory authorities. An important issue i s also how to ensure maximum
protection o f small depositors and retail investors, in particular, by fostering the highest
transparency in accounting and corporate governance standards.

3.51        Another regulatory obstaclefor efficient intermediation is thefact that thefinancial
system is not subjected to Brazil's Competition Law. Strengthening the oversight o f competition
inthe financial sector is also apriority, considering the recordhighmargins consistently reported
by Brazilianbanks inrecent years (average return on equity, ROE, was inthe range of 25 percent
in 2004). While high spreads may in part reflect structural inefficiencies as mentioned above
(e.g., highfundingrates, significant taxes, and operational costs), market structure and barriers to
entry will have to be more closely monitored to ensure a competitive and affordable provision of
financial services to the population at large.

3.52         Under existing regulations, the Central Bank is responsible for the oversight of
mergers and acquisitions as well as market conduct issues in the banking ~ector.~'                                             The role o f
the antitrust authorities is limited to other sectors. However, inconsistent case law has blurred
this division o f responsibilities in the past.42In 2001, the Central Bank's exclusive authority to
approve all mergers and acquisitions o f financial institutions in Brazil has been confirmed in a
highlevel legal opinion.43Inpractice, the effectiveness of competition and antitrust oversight in
the banking sector i s currently limited. Existing resolutions on bank mergers and acquisitions
provide for almost automatic approval o f transactions by the Central Bank.44

3.53        As the financial system grows in size and complexity and becomes more integrated
with global markets, sound legislation for systemic risk control will become more of a
necessity. A clear-cut division o f responsibilities among the Central Bank and other supervisory
authorities appears to be important as well as increasing the resources available for oversight of
payment systems and risk-based supervision o f financial markets and institutions.

3.54        A coherent development strategyfor financial deepening would also require a number
of measures to strengthen the sector's institutional infrastructure. Modern credit reporting
systems, for example, contribute to fostering a financial culture where creditworthiness becomes
the most important asset. Transparent and wide ranging availability o f credit information would
allow banks to expand their lending to the private sector and in particular to individuals and
SMEs, mobilizing large amounts of deposits that are so far invested mostly in Government

assets. More integrated payment systems would allow a larger share o f the population to access
electronic means o fpayment, reducing costs.


MobilizingLong-TermResources

3.55       Brazil is probably the only major Latin American country that still requires its banks
to allocate a material portion of certain liability classes to specific economic sectors such as
housing and agriculture. Directed credit in Brazil has included the earmarking o f 25 percent of
sight deposits for agriculture and 65 percent of savings deposits for housing finance.45There has
been a further mandatory allocation o f 2 percent o f demand deposits for micro-finance.

3.56       Federal banks continue to play an important role in the banking sector and have a
proportionately lower contribution to intermediation. The top five federal banks accounted for
roughly 41 percent o f total assets, 42 percent o f total deposits, and 38 percent o f total loans in
June 2003. Following a restructuring process in June 2001, federal banks' interest income from
loans is proportionally lower than for private banks (only 43.8 percent compared to 61 percent
from the top five private banks), with the difference being generated mainly by substantial
securities income (46.5 percent o f interest income). Capital adequacy o f the top federal banks,
albeit still below private banks, has improved remarkably following their recapitalization in
2001. For example, Banco do Brasil now has a Bank for International Settlements (BIS) ratio of
17.4 percent (as o f September 2005) and the Caixa Econbmica Federal o f 18.4 percent.

3.57       Although Brazil's capital markets were the largest in Latin America in absolute size,
they play a limited role in the provision of long term finance. Brazil's history of
macroeconomic instability, very high real interest rates, large Government borrowing needs,
extensive public intervention in the market for long term capital, and a taxation regime that has
hurt liquidity of many forms o f financial contracts have all contributed to the emasculation of
local capital markets. Coupled with worldwide economic shocks and the bursting o f the
technology bubble, the reasons behindfew recent substantial IPOs and the under-development o f
venture capital become evident.

3.58       Pension funds and insurance companies could provide long term finance to the
private sector at favorable terms, contributing to financial innovation and promoting sound
corporate governance. In Brazil, the contributions o f pension funds and insurance companies to
financial intermediation and growth has yet to achieve its potential, due, among other reasons, to
their moderate size relative to banks, the partial success in increasing membership and
mobilizing resources, under-fundingproblems in closed pension funds, and excessive financing
o f the public sector (which accounts for roughly half o f their portfolios). Inorder to fulfill their
potential roles, these institutional investors must strengthen their capacity to invest and manage
risk.The pension fund and insurance sectors in Brazil have estimated assets accounting for 23.5
percent o f GDP in 2005. The Government has been implementing policies designed to promote




  Of which 80 percent(52 percent of the total) are at below market interest rates, for low income(SFH) housing.



                                                                      30

the sound growth o f institutional investors, and enable them to play a more important role in
financial intermediati~n.~~

3.59         Brazil's insurance sector accountsfor over a third of total insurance assetsfor Latin
America.47Its total premium amounts to 2.8 percent o f GDP, with roughly US$ 16 billion in
assets. Although smaller than the pension funds, the insurance sector i s another important source
of long term finance inmature economies. Total insurance premium amounted to an average o f 9
percent o f GDP for industrialized countries in 2004. Thus, significant room exists for further
growth o fthe Brazilian insurance industryas a source o f longterm finance for the economy.

3.60         Despite its importance, the insurance market's performance in Brazil has been
characterized by rapidly increased costs and low innovation. Distribution and administrative
costs are higher in Brazil (equivalent to 34 percent o f total premiums in 1998) than in the U.S.
(25 percent) and the U.K.(21 percent). The market has been traditionally dominated by non-life
insurance and, in particular, motor vehicle insurance. New life insurance products are being
launched following an increasing demand by pension funds. However, a large share o f resources
inthe insurance industry continues to bechanneled to finance the public sector.

3.61         One reason for limited technical skills and product innovation is the existence of the
reinsurance monopoly held by the Instituto de Resseguros do Brasil (IRB). All insurance
companies inBrazil must turn to IRB first for their reinsurance needs and, only ifauthorized, can
tap the international reinsurance market. Legislation proposing a gradual opening o f the
reinsurance market i s currently being discussed in Congress. As the insurance market grows in
size and complexity, so will the reinsurance needs. With limited fiscal space, the reinsurance
capacity o f the public monopoly will increasingly show its limits. Leveraging private sector
experience and sophistication in managing risks, while stepping up regulatory oversight, will be
the key to ensure a sound and rapid growth o f the insurance and reinsurance sectors inBrazil.

3.62         I n forging an efficient insurance sector in Brazil significant restructuring would be
necessary. This includes opening-up the market for new firms, transferring regulatory powers
over the reinsurance sector fi-om IRB to an insurance regulator, and improving corporate
governance in IRB. The IRB itselfhas been in an improving financial position. Uncertainty and a
lack o f recruitment have meant that personnel with relevant expertise have been progressively
eroding, From time to time, consideration has been given to some degree o f liberalization in the
IRB monopoly, by allowing other entities into the domestic market. Indications have been that a
significant number o f large global reinsurers would seek to establish operations inBrazil.

3.63         The Superintendtncia de Seguros Privados (SUSEP), the supervisor for the
insurance sector, has made impressiveprogress over the preceding five years to improve both

mortality table. Technical capacity within SUSEP is, however, in need o f development. Efforts
are ongoing to adjust supervisory procedures in line with new IAIS principles, the enhancement
o f the valuation and solvency regime in the light o f emerging new accounting standards for
insurance, and the enhancement of disclosure.


Expanding FinancialAccess

3.64      Although Brazil is not noticeably financially underserved relative to other middle-
income countries, the pace, equity, and efficiency of growth could be expanded by greater
dissemination offinancial services to small enterprises and to thepoorest segments of society.
Issues relatingto this have beendetailed indepthin a recent Bank

3.65       Financial services are unevenly distributed geographically and among enterprises in
Brazil. Studies on growth constraints found that more than half o f small and medium-sized
enterprises view the lack of financing as the main impediment to their expansion. It i s estimated
that about 10 percent o f micro-enterprises and 20 percent of small enterprises have access to
external sources o f financing and that the bulk o f financing to medium and small firms comes
fiom public banks or microfinance institutions indirectly supported by public banks at subsidized
rates, Despitethis low lending, default rates are as highas 15 percent.

3.66      Larger firms have more access to credit than micro-firms, even after controlling for
other firm characteristics. Being a firm with more than 500 employees increases the probability
o f having a loan by 25 percentage points compared to being a micro-firm (less than 20
employees). Being a medium-sized firm (100-499 employees) increases the probability o f having
a loan by 17percentage points compared to being a m i ~ r o - f i r m . ~ ~

3.67       There is also evidence that access to finance by individuals is strongly affected by
income levels. Individual characteristics are more important than location and financial
institutions' characteristics. Among the individual characteristics, income level is even more
important than education. Moreover, income level appears to be the single relevant determinant
o f the amount of credit demanded and received by individuals. In other words, richer people
demand and receive higher amounts o f credit than poorer people. For example, only two percent
o f the poorest 10 percent of the Brazilian population demanded and received credit in the early
2000s, while this percentage increased to 25 percent among the richest 10 percent o f the
Brazilian p o p u l a t i ~ n . ~ ~

D.         Transforming Knowledge into Productivitv Gains and Innovation

3.68         Brazil has been a pioneer of science and technology policies in Latin America with
early investments generating some important results. The country had an explicit federal
science and technology (S&T) policy since 1969. Brazil has its own Ministry o f Science and
Technology (MCT) and a National Council on Science and Technology (CCT), aiming at
defining S&T strategies and coordinating inter-Government initiatives. It has established two
strong federal institutions to promote basic research and several research institutes have
international reputation for their e~cellence.~'Brazilian scientists were the first to crack the
genetic code o f the Xvlella fastidiosa bacteria that attacks orange trees and vines. World-class
technology programs have been developed in aeronautics (Embraer), satellites (CBERS),
biotechnolo y (Genoma),
(Petrobras).      ii                           tropical agriculture (Embrapa),                  and deep-water exploration

technology absorption are not as good as expected. Most a gregate output measures o f
innovation indicate that Brazil i s in a low to intermediate position.'                           Analysis by the World Bank
suggests that on U.S. patents and scientific publications, Brazil has historically under-performed
similar economies by about 80 percent.54Results are better in terms o f technology absorption,
but there is scope for improvement: trade of capital goods was lower b international standards
and even licensing agreements play more important roles elsewhere.'                                        World Bank research
shows only 2 percent o f Brazilian firms would consider licensing operations from international
sources among their three main sources of technology acquisition versus 24 percent o f Chinese
firms.56

3.71        Incentives for public R&D were misaligned in at least two main ways. First, cost-
effective output-oriented research in not duly rewarded. Second, nor i s the transfer o f public-
sector generated knowledge to the private sector encouraged for commercial application.
Seventy-five percent o f the Brazilian R&D efforts are aimed at "general knowledge
improvement" as opposed to any specific activity, an emphasis on basic research that i s much
higher than that found inMexico (4 percent) Spain (7 percent) or Portugal (25 percent), countries
with arguably comparable cultural heritage. International evidence shows that ifsuch spending is
not submitted to the appropriate incentive regime, university R&D will be ineffective in
producing productive application^.^^

3.72        Public resources allocated through "Sector Funds" helped generatefunding for S&T,
but their design wasfragmented and their implementation cumbersome. The Verde-Amarelo
Fundand the Infrastructure Fundhave better defined objectives: to improve cooperation between
the private-sector and universities and to support modernization o f R&D infrastructure,
respectively. These two funds are free of constraints on resource allocation by sector and use
subsidized loans and risk-sharing instrument^.^^ One major limitation o f the use o f Sector Funds
i s the impossibility o f directly transferring resources from the public to the private sector to
support R&D, the reason why most funds had to go through sometimes complex triangulations
with universities and public institutions.


58Universitiesand Governmentresearchcentersaccounted for more than half R&D expenditures in Brazil in 2000. The fact that Embmpaobtains
a significantpart of its researchbudget through competitivebidding has been argued to be one important reason for its R&D effectiveness. See
Beintema, N.M.et al. (2001). "Agricultural R&D in Brazil - Policy, Investments and Institutional Profile." Washington, D.C.: IFPRI-Embrapa-
Fontagro.

59A further instrument is support to technology-oriented companies through the Conrec-Condiminurn risk-sharingprogram of BNDESPAR (a
subsidiary BNDES).



                                                                    34

3.73         Brazil also offered tax incentives for private R&D similar to those in developed
countries (e.g., accelerated depreciation and carry-forward provisions). Tax-breaks to private
R&D are provided by at least five different laws, amounting to RS$ 1.83 billion in 2003 (three
times higher than the 1995-value), o fwhich roughly 84 percent (RS$1.53 billion) were applied to
IT industries by Law No. 8,248/1991. Sector concentration and design issues make the overall
impact o f these tax-breaks unclear on R&D costs. Since 2000, sixteen sector funds have been
created totaling RS$ 1.59 million in 2005. These sector fbnds have the objective o f providing
stable public R&D funds. They are disbursed by FINEP, mainly as grants, with diverse
objectives, recipients, and duration.60 Disbursements have been unstable and slow and the laws
governing them generally created obstacles to their optimal use.61

3.74         Intellectual Property Rights (IPR) were a further source of low innovative output.
Low private R&D investments may be due to low expected net private returns. IPR laws in
Brazil are still inadequate despite recent improvements (especially regarding licensingprocesses,
simplified in 1993). The National Institute for Property Rights (INPI) still lacks appropriate
human and financial resources and delays remain an issue.

3.75         Overall, Brazil lacked a clear technology and innovation policy and a better incentive
regimefor technology absorption and the efficient use of existing knowledge stock and used
inputs. A coherent set o f measures focusing on a few well-defined objectives i s essential. A
common vision on how to translate public investment in knowledge into productivity and
innovation i s necessary.62A better incentive regime will require: (i)a well structured set o f
incentives for knowledge transfer from the public to the private sector; (ii)surpassing the
limitations for direct transferring o f public funds to private R&D; and (iii)                                  a rationalization o f the
use o f sector funds, possibly towards their consolidation and governance improvement; and the
monitoring and evaluation o f tax incentives. IPR enforcement i s also needed but it is part o f a
broader set o f institutionalreforms that Brazil needs to go through and for which implementation
should be considered a long term challenge.

represents the first global environmental trading scheme, with the objective to reduce
greenhouse gas emissions.63The Kyoto Protocol became effective in February 2005 after the

3.77       The uncertainties of whether there will be a second commitmentperiod after 2012 and
the rather slow and non-transparent registration processfor projects are considered to be the
main impediments for a quick market development. At XI Session o f the Conference o f the
Parties to the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (COP-11) held in
Canada in December 2005, the international community decided that negotiations for a second
commitment period will start immediately and there will be procedural improvements in the
approval system o f projects at the international level. The expectation that market mechanisms
will continue after 2012 will certainly stimulate the private sector to offer new projects and
Brazil has the potential to further benefitfiom the continuity o f this market.

3.78      Indeed, Brazil's project pipeline already offered to the market is significant compared
to other development countries. The Brazilian Interministerial Commission for Global Climate
Change (ICCC), the Designated National Authority (DNA) responsible for the approval and
promotion o f CDM projects, complied with the market expectations and its challenges. But, in
order to take full advantage o f market potentials, further support will be necessary and the DNA
restructuringshould be considered to further efficiency increases.

3.79      ICCC approval process, in particular, should be further streamlined. The
responsibility o f approval of validated projects should be delegated to a sub-commission that
would meet more frequently. A decentralized approach o f the evaluation o f Project Design
Documents (PDDs) through a sub-commission i s also recommendable. The delegation o f this
task would not only accelerate the approval process, but also free time for ICCC's roles as a
coordination unit and policy-maker o f the Brazilian climate change policy. It should be further
considered whether a qualified sub-commission would also permit the presentation o f original
documentation in English.The translation of PDDs and validation reports into Portuguese is seen
by project developers as time consuming and costly, leading to further delays inthe preparation
and approval processes o f C D M projects inBrazil.

3.80      Moreover, the monitoring system should be improved. A comprehensive re-modeling
should permit an accurate accompanying o f the implementation o f the C D M projects and o f the
actual emission reductions over time, based on the verification reports. This monitoring activity
will also contribute to an improvement o f data for climate change policy formulation and
planning instruments.

3.81      Another issue to be addressed is the capacity building of key stakeholders on the CDM
market. The dissemination o f information on C D M project cycles and technical aspects i s

as result, the overall costs of CDMproject design.

                         4. THE PROGRAMAND THE PROPOSEDLOAN

A.    ' The Promammatic Framework

4.1      Theproposed loan ispart of aprogram that encompasses a set o f initiatives, addressing
an integrated and technically coherent set o f microeconomic measures and institutional reforms
(the Growth Program) judged both necessary and sufficient to foster economic growth and
reduce poverty. This program i s justified by the overall performance o f the Brazilian
administration, in terms o f strong macroeconomic management, development o f participatory
democratic institutions, innovative social programs, and commitment to microeconomic reforms
for growth.

4.2      Theprogram supports a balanced set of structural legal and institutional measures. A
feature o f the growth agenda is that it consists not only o f passing laws (or constitutional
amendments, etc.), but also o f (i)defining approaches to public-private interactions; (ii)
strengthening the institutional capacity o f regulatory agencies to plan and regulate; and, (iii)
enforcing laws and regulations consistently and predictably. This demands the exercise o f
judgment in appropriately definingthe triggers o f loan operations. There i s a trade-off between
insisting on "observables" such as laws or the formation o f agencies" and "less tangible"
measures, such as the definition o f an approach in a Government White Paper or the adoption of
hiringnorms in an agency. These, taken together, may be more important for economic growth
than laws strictu sensu. The present loan contains a balance between these two types o f actions.
The importance o f the second type - or the Government effort involved - should not be
underestimated.

4.3      Brazil's legislative process dictates a flexible programmatic approach. Laws and
constitutional amendments must pass two stages o f voting in each House o f the Legislative
Branch, implying uncertainty over the passage speed o f each reform. This also means that the
Government does not fully control the pace o f reforms. Given the number o f fronts contained in
Brazil's growth agenda, this imposes a flexible programmatic approach.

4.4      The Growth Program initially comprisedfour areas to be developed over the next
following years, namely: (i)logistics; (ii)    business environment; (iii)  financial efficiency and
depth; and, (iv) innovation. The successful implementation o f this program i s not only desirable
initself to foster growth, given that it will also reinforcethe administration's adherence to sound
macroeconomic policies - a choice that, by itself, has incurred non-negligible political costs -
but it will also increase resources for poverty-reducing social programs. The Growth Program
forms a subset of a broader growth agenda, which includes other microeconomic reform areas
important for growth (e.g., trade, taxation, labor markets, and contract enforcement) - all o f them
subjected to Government consultations and policy development, but excluded from the present
loan.

4.5      The FPLSEG identified long term goals and an implied path of reforms in all four
areas, Key next steps were established and a set o f complementary measures - a critical mass o f
additional reform initiatives - that were also defined in each o f the four areas. The FPLSEG
supported institutional and policy reforms in the following areas and sub-areas: (i)        logistics:
customs administration, ports, road maintenance, and multimodality; (ii)      business environment:


                                                  38

infrastructure regulation, competition, business registration and operation, and corporate
insolvency network; (iii)financial efficiency and depth: financial competition, prudential
regulation, long term financing, and credit access; and, (iv) innovation and technology policy:
public R&D effectiveness private innovation and environmental markets.

4.6       Conditionshad been describedfor the second and third loans in accordance to reform
progress, Reform progress was not expected to occur at uniform speed across the four
components. Indeed, this would be unrealistic given their depth and scope. The Bank would,
thus, support subsets o f this reform agenda as the Government progresses inone or more areas.
The next (third) loan would be enabled when the key next steps (triggers) in one or several
program areas have been met and, at the same time, a critical mass o f reform measures from the
broader matrix has been achieved, such that there i s sufficient confidence in substantial progress
towards the development outcomes in terms o f equitable and sustainable growth.

4.7       Afirst case - of Uneven Reform Progress acrossPillars - was viewed as most likely at
the time the FPLSEG wasprepared. The second loan would be primarily based on progress in
the areas o f the business environment and financial sector reforms, with critical mass coming
mostly from the other two pillars. Within the business environment, triggers would be the
passing into law o f measures that have been launched, most notably the PPP law, the bankruptcy
law, and the amendment to the national tax code.66A career development plan for regulators
would also have passed into law.67A third loan would shift focus to the innovation system. The
enactment o f the new Innovation Law would be a trigger, encouraging greater private innovation
and interaction between public-sector research and private-sector development.68 Similarly,
improvements o f sector funds would make their allocation more transparent and competitive.
Finally, markets incarbon trade could, by this stage, be functioning on a sustainable basis.

4.8       Another case - of More Uniform but Slower Progress - was also consideredpossible.
This would see slower progress in the financial sector and the business environment
compensated for by faster progress in logistics and innovation and possibly with key measures
coming in new areas, such as trade integration and taxation. In the absence o f significant
alterations to financial sector regulation, progress on either the restructuring o f the sector funds
or the implementation o f the Innovation Law would work as trigger actions for which the second
loan may wait. The third loan would, then, depend on balanced progress across the pillars of the
program.

B.       PROCRESS SISCE THE FPLSEC:                EXPECTED      RESULTS FURTHER
                                                                               ASD           STEPS

4.9       I n the logistics sector, good progress was achieved toward reaching the main goals
established by the FPLSEG in three out of four sub-areas. No significant progress was made
on the port sector. The customs administration succeeded in reducing the average net release
time o f merchandise by 20 percent on average, and simplified export procedures, especially
through the expansion o f the Blue lane regime. In the road sector, the federal Government has
been gradually strengthening its road maintenance management capacity, with a draft policy

road network (compared to the 30 percent target established by the FPLSEG), and a concession
biddingprocess ready to be launched on another 5 percent of the network. The Government's
efforts to foster multimodal transport have met partial success, especially with the set-up o f a
fully operational PPP unit in the Ministry of Planning and the completion of the geographical
restructuring o f the railway concessions.

4.10         Other initiatives complement these key steps indicating the existence of a critical mass
of measures in the logistics field. Several clearance procedures have been simplified and/or
harmonized with those o f other agencies inthe past two years, and good progress has beenmade
in gradually modernizing the customs administration's management and information systems. In
the road sector, funding for road maintenance and rehabilitation was adequate (especially in
2005), and a career plan was established for the national department for transport planning
(DNIT), which will help to attract better qualified staff.69In turn, this allowed the first public
hiring for new DNIT staff to be launched, the first such hiring since 1994. In the field of
multimodal transport, good progress has been made, with particular emphasis on the regulation
of the railway sector. Even in the slower reforming port sector, the approval o f the "Agenda
Pmtos'', setting the stage for port reform, infrastructurerehabilitation, and implementation o f the
ISPS code was an important first step for the advancement o f changes in this area. A detailed
update on the progress o f reforms inthe logistics sector can be found inAnnex 4.

strengthening. The two main areas in which significant short term gains can be achieved at a
relatively low political cost are customs administration and road network management.
Increasing efficiency in clearance processes and border protection mainly requires a series of
operational and administrative decisions/actions which can be handled by the customs
administration itself. And in the road sector, most o f the reform's next steps have to do with
consolidating and formalizing agreements reached in principle (with the exception o f the
decentralization process), especially as the maintenance and rehabilitation o f the federal road
network i s a high-level priority for the G~vernment.~'The main directions for further reform in

4.12        All key next steps established by the FPLSEG in the field of business environment
were achieved. The Public-Private Partnership (PPP) law was approved by Congress in
December 2004, creating the appropriate legal environment for such joint investments. A draft
law on a career development plan for the staff o f regulatory agencies was not only submitted, as
projected, but also approved by Congress, a step expected for a later phase. The final approval o f
the Bankruptcy Law and o f the corresponding changes to the National Tax Code in December
2004 was pivotal: the reform process had been delayed for more than 10 years in Congress.
These two pieces align the bankruptcy legislation in Brazil with international best-practices,
improving market exit as well as the protection o f creditors' rights. With support o f the
Technical Assistance Loan, a series o f training and professional improvement courses on the new
bankruptcy regime was offered to judges and attorneys general.71A preliminary update on the
use o f the new bankruptcy regime by local firms can be found inAnnex 5.

4.13        A critical mass of initiatives was also undertaken in the field of competition and
simplifying business registry and operation.' An initiative named Pilot Project in Infrastructure
(PPI) was structure inthe Treasury Department to increase the quality and the quantity o f public
investments in infrastructure, while keeping fiscal responsibility. The PPP unit was established
under the Ministry o f Planning. A draft law that opens up the market for the transport o f natural
gas i s to be submitted to Congress.72A draft law that consolidates the Brazilian antitrust regime
was sent to Congress after five years o f public discu~sion.~~The reform o f the Brazilian antitrust
system had been established as key next steps for a third operation. Provisory Measure M P
255/2005 (known as the new "MP do Bern"), approved in October 2005, will, among other
things, raise the upper limit for micro and small enterprises to be part o f a program that
simplifies and reduces payments of federal taxes, while exempting firms that export at least 80
percent o f their gross revenues from the payment o f National Integration Program (PIS) and
Contribution for Social Use (Confins) over the acquisition o f machinery and equipment.74

4.14         The federal administration is currently developing a project to integrate tax
administration at the three levels of Government to streamline business registration. The
project on integration o f the revenue services at the three levels o f Government is underway and
is, thus, the one that will be completed in the short-run. Although not mandatory, it i s backed by

been sent to insurance companies to collect information and supervise their degree o f compliance

(Circ. 253/2004, 276/2004, and 285/2005). A detailed update on the progress o f reforms in the
financial sector can be found inAnnex 7.

4.18        A critical mass of initiatives was also undertaken in thefields of increasingfinancial

authority (CADE) and the BCB was signed, establishing a clearer division o f responsibilities in
the oversight o f competition issues." Innovative instruments have been proposed to channel
resources towards "special interest sectors," such as housing and public infrastructure, including
investment funds constituted as special purpose vehicles or venture capital funds." Two types o f
mutual funds have been conceived: (a) the Real Estate Investment Fund, and (b) the Credit
Rights Investment Fund. New measures on credit securitization mechanisms have been
approved, including Law No. 10,93 112004, which considers financing and securitization
instruments for the real estate market, comprising the Property Liens on Real-Estate
Developments, the Real Estate Letter of Credit and the Real-Estate Credit Bill (LCI).
Additionally, Law No. 10,931/2004 regulates the Bank Credit Bill (LCB), a securitized credit
instrument, backed by existing loans, which ensures expeditious resolution o f defaults. The

4.19       The use ofpositive credit information (Cadastro Positivo) and the payroll deduction
loans seem to have improved access to credit to the poor and SMEs. A new regulation was
drafted to enhance the use o f positive credit information. Reducing asymmetric information and
allowing banks to track negative and positive information on borrowers might in fact make credit
cheaper for the low-income population. The average annual rate on the payroll deduction loans
was 36.9 percent inDecember 2005, much lower than the average monthly rate on other personal
loans (60.4 percent).82 From January 2004 to May o f 2005, the volume o f payroll deduction
loans approximately doubled fiom around R$ 6 billion to approximately R$ 17.8 billion. 83 New
measures have recently been passed allowing all public sector employees to benefit from this
product. In November 2004, the Federal Government also launched the National Program of
Oriented Productive Micro Credit (PNMPO), aimed at expanding access to productive credit for
formal and informal micro- entrepreneur^.'^

4.20       Several agricultural insurance schemes and rural credit mechanisms became
available. These include the private sector agribusiness property and casualty insurance (Law
No, 10,823/2003 and decree 5,121/2004); Proagro Mais, a Government program within
PRONAF providing basic insurance to small farmers against adverse climate events (CMN
resolution No. 3,23412004); and, Garantia SAFRA, a shared-premium scheme to create an
insurance fund offering basic coverage for regions particularly subject to drought (e.g., the
Northeast).

4.21       Enhancing efficiency and depth of the financial system in Brazil will inevitably
require addressing important macro and microeconomic issues. The crowding out o f the
private sector due to excessive public debt, the implicit cost o f directed credit, and the role o f
BNDES and other public banks, although relevant, are not part o f the short-term agenda o f the
Government. Nevertheless, room for significant improvement exists inthe following directions:

          0    Increasing competition in the bank system, among other means, by extending the
               antitrust law to the financial sector;

          0    An enhanced interface among credit registries and a more direct linkage between
               credit reporting and payment systems would aide banks in their lending decisions
               while facilitating the BCB's supervisory role inthe financial sector;

          0    Mobilizing long-term resources by completing the process o f liberalization o f the
               insurance market;

          0    Expanding access through the use o f market-based institutions, including the

4.22       I n the field of technology and innovation, the approval of the Innovation Law
anticipated an achievement expected in a later phase of the program. This approval i s a very
important step towards a productive application o f the scientific knowledge available in Brazil.
The envisaged instruments include the possibility o f agreements between public entities and
private companies, the transfer o f technology, and sharing public infrastructure o f laboratories,
equipment, and instruments with private companies. It created the boundaries for the definition
o f the intellectual property rights distribution over inventions between researchers and research
institutions. The law also alters the concept o f "subven@o econ8mica" providing for direct
public support to private research and development (R&D), an essential step for the development
of a cost-effective subsidy regime for private R&D.

4.23       Complementary steps to improve the system of tax incentives and the governance of
Sector Funds were also taken, Inorder to stimulate investmentsin R&D o f new technologies, a
60 percent levy over expenditures in R&D is allowed in the total annual taxes to be paid by
firms, according to the "Lei do Bern." This legal piece also allows the provision o f subsidies o f
up to 50 percent o f the cost o f hiring researchers with post-graduate studies. In addition, the
Ministry o f Science and Technology (MCT) took several initial steps to rationalize the use of
resources available to finance R&D activities through the Sector Funds. M C T improved the
management o f the Funds by creating a technical secretariat and a managerial committee to
improve coordination and coherence. Several cross-cutting programs - as opposed to sector
specific - were created and each Fund will contribute with 50 percent o f its budget for these
actions.

4.24       The evolution of the carbon trading segment was very positive; particularly in 2005,
contributing with additional results to the composition of the required critical mass of the
program. Resolution No. 2 o f ICCC establishes the procedures for approval o f project activities
under the Clean Development Mechanism (CDM) and approves the procedures for afforestation
and reforestation project activities under the C D M o f the Kyoto Protocol. In 2005, 78 C D M
projects where approved by the ICCC (more than any other country in the world).*' ICCC also
decided to take advantage o f the pre-Kyoto market opportunities and permitted the approval
process to be implemented even before the treat entered into force. According to MCT, a total
amount of 130.2 million tons CO:! eq. i s expected to be reduced during the first commitment
period. One can estimate that, in this first phase, the Brazilian economy will benefit in the total
value o f about US$780 million.86

represents a huge potential for the Brazilian economy. It encompasses areas in which so-far
unexplored resources - namely, available knowledge in public research centers as well as public
funded R&D; natural resources, including the rainforest; and, bio-diesel technologies. The
institutional reforms achieved so far are solid first steps but, inorder to generate concrete results,

C.      The ProposedSecond ProgrammaticLoanand Possible Future Operations

4.27     The proposed operation (Second Growth Loan) would, first, consolidate reforms
implemented in the four areas originally selected by the FPLSEG, supporting achievements
reached by the Brazilian administration between 2004 and 2005. The FPLSEG (Board
presentation in January 2004) supported the design o f a program o f reforms that constituted a
new growth strategy for Brazil. It targeted key measures to boost private investment and
productivity, while avoiding inefficient microeconomic policies. Another loan (Board
presentation in June 2005) extended the Bank's support mainly to the housing sector, in which
the Government undertook several steps to create an efficient unified formal housing market
based on private capital-markets - a clear departure from a history o f directed credit and market
segmentation.

4.28     Macroeconomic management continues to the exemplary. As a result o f a resilient
commitment to sound macroeconomic management, fundamentals o f the Brazilian economy
showed further improvement since the first operation. The improvement o f macroeconomic
conditions allowed Brazil not to renew the Stand-By arrangement with the IMF as investor
confidence has increased. Moreover, the growing strength o f Brazil's external position allowed
the country to make an early repayment of its entire outstanding obligations to the institution.
Impressive results have been achieved interms o f the reduction o f sovereign spreads which have
reached its minimumhistorical levels at around 300 basis points in2005.

4.29     The agenda of microeconomic reforms is also being reflected in selective efforts to

4.30       Thepillars of this loan are the achievements expressed in the Table 4.1, which shows
considerable achievement in the conditions established for a second operation. Main legal
reforms since the FPLSEG include the approval by Congress o f the Public-Private Partnership
(PPP) Law, the Bankruptcy and Insolvency Law, and the corresponding amendments to the
National Tax Code.88Other quantitative triggers were reached in most o f the logistics cost sub-
sector and the financial sector. No action was expected inthe innovationfield.

4.3 1     Several other initiatives in each of thefour areas strengthened the development of the
program, providing for the required `%ritical mass" for the second operation. The Lei do Bern
raised the upper limit for SMEs to participate in a tax simplification program and exempted
exporting firms from the payment o f PIS and Cofins over the acquisition o f capital goods - a
benefit that should cost US$ 2.5 billion per year. The federal administration i s currently
developing a project aiming at integrating tax administration at the three levels o f Government to
streamline business registration. The use o f positive credit information (Cudustro Positivo) and
the payroll deduction loans seem to be improving credit access by the poor and SMEs.
Agricultural insurance schemes and rural credit mechanisms provided by the private sector
became available thanks to institutional changes introduced by the Government. Complementary
steps were taken to improve the systemo f tax incentives and the governance o f Sector Fundsthat
support private R&D, resources that added up to US$ 1.3 billion (0.2 percent o f GDP in2004).

4.32      Secondly, thisproposed loan would support the achievements of important additional
reforms, originally conceived as triggersfor a third operation. The enactment o f the Innovation
Law and the submission o f a Draft Law to reform the Brazilian antitrust system were also
attained.89A set o f measures to open-up the reinsurance market, medium-term actions according
to the FPLSEG, were also undertaken. A key law proposal (PLP 249/2005) has been presented to
Congress, regulating a gradual opening o f the reinsurance sector and reforming Instituto de
Reasseguros do Brusil (IRB) role and structure. SUSEP has made progress to improve the
quality o f the regulation and assume the role of supervisor o f the reinsurance sector, a better
institutional design for the sector.

4.33       Third, the proposed operation would reinforce the steps taken to improve judicial
performance, afield not originally supported by theprogram due to its complexity. This i s one
of the so-called "big ticket items" for which a long-term vision i s required. It was part o f the
broader set o f reform initiatives on which the Brazilian Government was working despite not
being considered by the FPLSEG. Three main dimensions o f the reform are o f interest o f this
loan: (i) reduce delays caused by excessive appeals and congested dockets; (ii) improve the
           to                                                                          to
enforcement o f judgments, especially in civil cases and to increase court efficiency and expand
access through ICT, administrative reorganizations, and (iii)    the addition o f alternative services
(e.g. small claims courts, mediation).

improve contract enforcement, a key component of the cost of doing business in Brazil. The
most important o f these measures i s the approval o f a constitutional amendment (Constitutional
Amendment No. 45 of 2004). It has the potential to improve the appeal system by making

4.35        The general belief is that no single change will alter judicial performance
significantly and, thus, that this collection of measures will all be required. Within months o f
the CE 45's passage, the Ministry o f Justice sent 26 reform bills to the Congress, all intended to
eliminate the bottlenecks its experts had identified in the civil, labor, and criminal proceedings.
The challenge i s to end a series o f interrelated practices with deep structural and behavioral
roots, and from which most o f the major players derive some benefit. It i s anticipated that
measures to reduce delays caused by appeals and congested dockets; and the provision for the
addition o f alternative services (e.g. small claims courts, mediation) will proceed most rapidly. In
the meantime, there i s also ample room for the introduction of organizational and technological
innovations to help courts move their cases more rapidly. A detailed update on the progress of
reforms inthejudicial sector can be found inAnnex 8.

defined in either cases established by the First Loan (Le,, "Uneven Reform Progress Across
Pillars" and "More Uniformbut Slower Progress") for a follow-up operation." Annex 2 includes

4.37     Subject to reform progress, a third loan is envisaged as part of the original program.
A third operation will be enabled when Government has made substantial overall progress in
implementation o f its Growth Program, as described in Annex 3. Within this broader program,
the Bank's operation focuses particularly on the indicative key next steps summarized in the
third column o f the Bank Matrix (Annex 2). These indicative key next steps form the basis on
which progress will be assessed and the prior actions for a third operation will be determined.

4.38     The Bank's decision to proceed with a third loan will, therefore, be based on

program 's broad goals - reduced logistics costs, improved business environment, a more
implementation of indicative next steps which, together, make significant contributions to the

efficient and deep financial sector and increased innovation capacity. Substantial progress
might be achieved, for instance, interms of improving the business environment and enhancing
financial depth and efficiency. In the financial sector, key next steps are the approval by
Congress o f the insurance sector reform, which would help to mobilize longterm resources; and
the implementation o f positive information credit reporting (Cadastro Positivo), which would
improve access to credit by SMEs and the poor. In the field o f business environment, key next
steps are the consolidation o f the Conselho Nacional de Justiqa and the enactment of P L 90/2005
(Szimula Vinculante), major steps towards the implementation o f the judicial reform and the
improvement o f contract enforcement by thejudiciary.

4.39     Brazil's legislative process and the upcoming elections dictate aflexible programmatic
approach, Laws and constitutional amendments must pass two stages o f voting in each House of
the Legislative Branch, implying that some reforms will progress faster in some areas than
others. Moreover, the inauguration o f a new administration in 2007 will create the opportunity
for enlargement/adjustment o f the program. This could come from the inclusion o f issues o f the
broader Growth Agenda, such as trade policy and labor reform; or from the broader
Development Strategy, such as improving governance (see Figure 3.1). In either case,
adjustments should further strengthen the program goals and its overall outcomes, namely
increase potential output through private investments and productivity.


D.      Monitoring and Evaluation: Goals and Outcomes


4.40     The overarching goal defined in the FPLSEG was a sustained increase in potential
output growth based on higher investment and productivity gains, and leading to at least a
proportionate rise in the income of the poor. Where possible we have also defined monitorable
intermediate indicators for the main categories o f reform included in the first loan. These are
reproduced inthe fifth column o f the table in Annex 2.

4.41     Although short-term growth was relatively disappointing, improvements in growth
fundamentals are unquestionable. Output growth in 2004-2005 averaged 3.9 percent, in part
due to a 2005 slowdown accentuated by exogenous factors. Investment, however, maintained an
upward trend with total factor productivity and labor productivity improving significantly.
Investments reached one o f the highest levels since 1999 and its composition improved towards a


                                                50

larger share o f private investment in machinery and equipment. Total factor productivity
increased 1.4 percent in 2004 its best result for the last ten years while industrial labor
productivity increased by 15 percent since June 2003.

4.42        Evidence also indicates that growth has continued to benefit the poor; with a
sustained decreasein poverty and inequality rates. Poverty reduced from 27.26 percent to 25.08
percent from 2003 to 2004.92Total, rural, urban, and metropolitan extreme poverty falling by 8.0
percent, 6.5 percent, 8.2 percent, and 8.3 percent, respectively. With respect to income
redistribution, between 2001 and 2004, the income share appropriated by the richest 10 percent
of the population decreased from 47.3 percent to 44.7 percent. At the same time, the income
share o f the poorest 50 percent o f the population increased from 12.4 percent to 14.1 percent.
Finally, the remaining 40 percent o f the population earned a larger share o f income, too, and its
share increased from 40.0 percent to 41.2 percent.

4.43        While this outcome should not be solely attributed to recent economic growth, data
indicates that the pattern of growth has been more favorable to the poor (pro-poor).
Unemployment declined substantially to 9.6 percent in 2004 and 8.3 percent in 2005, the best
results since 2001. Low-skilled formal employment and real earnings also rose during this
period. According to the national household survey, 1.99 million and 1.52 million formal jobs
were created in 2004 and 2005, respectively. Average earnings also increased in real terms with
the largest increases (4.4 and 13.5 percent) going to those inthe informal sector.

4.44        The first operation defined a result-oriented framework in which key measures,
progress indicators and target outcomes were identified for each reform area. In logistics, the
main targets would be deep cuts in the through times in customs and ports to ease trade and
improve productivity. Within the business environment, an important target was to halve the
average time taken to register a new business. The number o f cartel prosecutions would also
provide an indication of progress in improving antitrust activity. The main measures o f success
o f financial reforms would be falling intermediation spreads and larger access to credit. In
innovation, a higher share of private R&D and a value o f trade under the Clean Development
Mechanism o f roughly US$ 100 million were some of the outcome indicators previously defined.

4.45        Even though the program outcomes for development policy operations can be
evaluated only at the end of the program, preliminary evidence is favorable so far. The net
release time o f customs clearance for exports has been declining with the volume o f trade
increasing three percentage points to reach 35 percent o f GDP in a period of currency
e v a l ~ a t i o n .While no major progress inthe time to register a firm has been measured so far, the
                     ~ ~
antitrust authority has successfully prosecuted at least 3 nationally relevant hard-core cartels. The
credit volume augmented from 25 to 28 percent o f GDP in less than two years, with the amount
of payroll deduction loans reaching US$ 5.45 billion and despite the fact that interest rates
started reducing only during the last months o f 2005. On innovation, it i s too early to find
indications o f the impact o f the reforms on private R&D but the volume o f carbon trading i s

4.46         I n addition, Bank analysis supports a positive evaluation of the FPLSEG. An
Implementation Completion Report (ICR) for the first operation was completed in June 2005.
The ICR was reviewed by the Bank's Independent Evaluation Group and the classifications
achieved by the different components varied between satisfactory and highly sati~factory.~~

                            Didconditions for economic growthimprove between 2004-2005?

Despite the disappointing performance o f 2005, evidence suggests that overall conditions for sustainable
economic growth in Brazil have improved since last operation. As discussed by the FPLSEG, the disappointing
growth dividend from the 1990s reforms has its roots in both slow capital accumulation and limited productivity
growth (labor productivity and PTF grew reasonably in the nineties, in part because employment fell). Gross annual
capital formation decreased from 23.5 percent o f GDP in the mid-seventies to less than 15 percent in the early
nineties, related largely to a decline in infrastructure investments. Productivity performance has been relatively
better as a result o f the structural reforms, in particular trade liberalization.

Investment ratios have shown systematic signs o f recovery and the savings constraint seems not to be binding.
Since the second quarter o f 2003, the share o f fixed capital gross formation to GDP) have increased from 16.36 to
20.43 percent inthe third quarter o f 2005. Although investment levels are subject to measurement controversies, the
recovery trend is confirmed regardless the indicator used.95Current levels are still low compared to India, China and
even Chile, Brazil's large competitors, but confirm a reversion in an eight-year downward trend. The evolution of
the capacity utilization since 2004 also suggests an optimistic expectation for the future.96

Savings are not constraining higher investment levels but the cost o f investment has been an important
limitation. On the one hand, the savings constraint, historically considered relevant to Brazilian growth, seem not to
be binding: domestic savings increased substantially, reaching 23 percent o f GDP and external savings are likely to
increase in the next years (i.e the country has generated current account surplus inthe last three years that would fall
in the next years). One the other, relative price o f capital goods increased continuously since 1999 without any
reversal movement untilearly 2005.97 It is reasonable to expect, however, that the exchange rate appreciation o f the
last two years and Government initiatives towards the reduction o f the taxation o f machinery and equipments will
induce the fall o f the price o f machinery and equipment.
The recent evolution o f total factor productivity also indicates an improvement in Brazil's growth potential.
The average total factor productivity growth since 2000 was 0.5%. In 2004, given its pro-cyclical nature, it jumped
to 1.5 percent value which is similar to the growth rate o f productivity o f the US. Assuming a TFP average growth
of 1.5 percent for the next years, the potential growth rate o f the Brazilian economy would be similar to the one
obtained in 2004 (5 percent),
The strong increase o f formal employment in the last three years is strengthening the contribution o f labor to
growth. Labor productivity increased by 15% since June 2003 and this increase was accompanied by an increase 01
6 percent in industrial employment. The increase o f productivity in the last two years contrasts with the increase

E.      R i s k s and Mitigation

4.47     As was the casefor thefirst operation, it should be recognized that theproposed loan
remains subject to moderate risk. Despite the fact that prior actions needed for this loan have
been achieved and a sufficient set o f complementary measureshave been undertaken, progress o f
the overall program is subject to moderate risks. Contributing to this fact i s the exclusion o f
certain "big ticket" items and the 2006 elections. On the positive side, recent macroeconomic
developments made the Brazilian economy less vulnerable to external shocks, an historical
source o f economic instability inthe country.

4.48     Original concerns about the effect of the exclusion of certain areas on the final
outcome of the operation seem to be less relevant by now. An energy shortage - despite the fact
that the program did not directly support reforms in the power sector - seems highly unlikely
until2009. Still, the set of microeconomic reforms hasbeen gradually extended, as illustratedby
the case o f the measures to improve house financing (supported by the Housing loan) and
contract enforcement by thejudiciary (to be supported by this loan).

4.49      The risks that the 2006 presidential elections change economic policies should be
balanced against the increasing perception that the economic strategy has started to generate
concrete results. As has been providedthroughout this report, there is ample evidence o f positive
impacts on economic activity and poverty alleviation o f the current development strategy. The
combination       o f strict  macroeconomic    management,    aggressive    social  policies   and
microeconomic reforms i s reducingunemployment, and poverty and helping to lessen inequality.
This raises the opportunity costs ofpolicy change.

4.50     The risk that the program may suffer from discontinuity should be balanced against
the demonstration of stability during the last political transition. Brazil has established a solid
precedent during the 2003 administrative transition in the field o f macroeconomic policies in a
moment o f high expectations o f change. Comparatively, the growth program i s less controversial
while impetus for radical policy changes i s lower.


F.      Linkswith CAS and WBGActivities

4.51     The Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) discussed by the Board on December 9, 2003
proposes Bank support towards a more equitable, sustainable, and competitive Brazil, built
upon a foundation of good governance and macroeconomic stability (Figure 4.1). The
proposed loan program forms a key part of this strategy. The main objective o f the loan is
supporting the implementation o f microeconomic foundations o f sustainable and equitable
economic growth in Brazil. It pursues a more competitive Brazil through reduced logistics and
financial costs, increased investment through better regulation, competition, business and
financial services, and increased productivity through innovation.

support to Brazil and one of the four pillars o f the CAS (together with sound fiscal policy; health
and education and environment). This series o f programmatic loans supports the third pillar o f
the CAS ("A More Competitive Brazil"). The second loan of this programmatic series
contributes to specific outcomes identified inthe CAS matrix related to its third pillar as detailed

recent operation under the fiscal series supports a pension reform sent to Congress in 2003.

environmental loan. Progress in the fields of education, health and social protection policies are
supported by the Second HD Programmatic loan, being proposedjointly with this operation.
