                                                                    98

Formal approval by Congress o f the antitrust law for the Banking Sector remains the key next
step supported by this loan within the goal o f enhancing competition in the financial sector. On
the other hand, the de-facto agreement already reached by CADE and the Central Bank appears
an achievement well above the short-term actions expected inthe previous operation.

As the Anti-trust Authority is currently not equippedto handle a possibly significant increase in
financial sector competition cases, the implementation o f this new antitrust regime for the
banking sector might prove challenging. A further problem stems from the fact that the new
agreement allows filing M&A transactions with the anti-trust authorities both a priori and a
posteriori. In practice, 99 percent o f mergers and acquisitions filings occur a posteriori and the
authorities may have no capacity to unwind completed transactions. Furthermore, the notion o f
`good functioning' of the financial sector i s not well defined in the new agreement, leaving the
Central Bank with a large degree o f discretion. While this provides flexibility for the analysis o f
systemically important transactions, it could limit the transparency o f Central Bank decisions and
open a window for political influence.

The Government has established a study group including the Central Bank, CADE, SDE and
SEAE to draft a code of conduct for mergers and acquisitions of financial institutions. The focus
inthe near future should be on capacity building in CADE and coordinating the roles of all the
agencies. Finally, particular care should be taken in evaluating the impact o f mergers and
acquisitions on the level o f competition inindividual market segments and geographic regions.

Inorder to further promote competitioninfinancial services inBrazil, a studyis beingconducted
by Bank staff, in collaboration with the Central Bank, to analyze the structure o f the financial
services industry, key players, revenue sources and issues in relevant market segments, and
assess the level o f competition in those market segments. Almost all previous studies o f
competition inBrazil have focused on aggregate data, while it i s recognized that the competitive
dynamics in some lines o f business and geographic regions are significantly more advanced than
in others. Some markets may show network economies and brand relevance, while others may
follow a process more similar to an auction. The analysis o f the microstructure o f financial
services will help the authorities identify priorities in their effort to ensure wide and affordable
access to financial services across the country.


Developments in credit reporting systems

Brazil's centralized credit risk database (SCR) has recently been enhanced and is increasingly
being used by bank supervisors as a monitoring tool. The removal o f residual difficulties in the
development o f the Credit Risk Center - which had been identified among the short-term actions
supported by the previous operation - i s now complete. The upgraded version (SCR 1.2) offers
increased storage capabilities, faster response time and higher data quality. It includes more
detailed information on the borrowers and a more user friendly system for both supervisors and
bankers with a web-based interface. Its coverage has expanded considerably in recent years and
now includes all credit transactions in amounts larger than 5,000 Reais. As a tool in lending
decisions, banks can search the SCR system to find out for example what credit limit potential
clients have obtained by competitor banks. As a supervisory tool, the SCR system can be used



                                                 99

instead to compare for instance loan classification systems across individual banks for
consistency. The Central Bank has also used SCR in conjunction with the deposit insurance fund
to price and buy portions o f bank loan books in order to inject liquidity in the banking system
when needed.

Another initiative which could significantly contribute to expand access to finance i s the new
draft regulation for the enhanced use o f positive credit information (Cadastro Positivo).
Reducing asymmetric information and allowing banks to track not only negative but also
positive information on borrowers might in fact make credit cheaper for the low-income
population. This i s because - in the absence o f real or personal guarantees - a positive credit
history i s the only clear asset that low-income borrowers can show in order to obtain affordable
lending rates. The approval by Congress of the Cadastro Positivo Law i s the key next step inthe
development o f Brazilian credit reporting systems. This will pave the way for the enhanced use
of positive credit information, which remains a medium-term goal supported by this operation.
The breadth of coverage of credit reporting systems, and inparticular the extent to which more
credit information will become available on individuals and SMEs facilitating access, represent
key outcome indicators.

The interlinkage among credit registries - which had been planned as a short-term action
supported by the previous operation - i s not yet complete. An enhanced interface among credit
registries and a more direct linkage between credit reporting and payment systems are important
for banks in their lendingdecisions while facilitating the Central Bank's supervisory role in the
financial sector. Key next steps for the future include also improvements o f accounting and
corporate governance standards which are currently under discussion.


Enhance Supervision of the Insurance Sector and Gradual Opening of the Re-Insurance
Market

Supervisory Framework

SUSEP, the supervisor for the insurance sector, has made dramatic progress in recent years to
improve both the quality o f the regulation and the effectiveness o f supervisory processes.

Several initiatives have been promoted in the area o f risk-based regulation and supervision
including the introduction o f actuarial tables, the definition o f the responsibilities o f the actuary
and the successful creation o f a policy registry.'43 Additionally, questionnaires have been sent to
insurance companies to collect information and supervise their degree o f compliance with rules
on a) underwriting risks; b) internal controls and c) operational and accounting risks (Circ. 253
and 276/2004 and 285/2005).

The objective o f strengthening customer education and disclosure - which was among the short-
term action supported by the previous operation - has substantially been achieved. Rules on
Internal Controls (Circ. 24912004) and the Committee o f Auditing (Res. 118/2004 and Circ.


14'Res.CNSP lI4/04, 118/04, 8/05, 121105and Circs. SUSEP 249/04,280/04,287/05



                                                               100

280/2004) have been introduced to strengthen corporate governance overall. Enhanced
transparency has been achieved through public hearings prior to the enactment o f new regulation
(Res. 8/2005 and delib. 92/2004), and greater consumer protection through the internal handling
of consumer complaints via the figure o f a company ombudsman (Res. CNSP 108/04, 110/04
and 127/05). SUSEP is also encouraging higher transparency and efficiency in the insurance
regulatory framework through a substantial simplification o f norms and procedures, and the
publication o f a monthly bulletin. It has also issued simplified procedures to achieve supervisory
approval o f corporate statutes (Circ. 26012004) and detailed rules for selfycertification o f
insurance professionals (Res. 115/2004 and Circ. 290/2004).

Additionally, new regulation has been passed granting tax relief for life insurance. (Decree
5,172/04).The norm gradually reduces the IOF tax on life insurance products with complete
removal by year 2006. A number o f additional measures have been passed to encourage the
development o f long-term savings and pension lans, by reducing both the fiscal burden and the
regulatory uncertainty for products inthis area.194

Looking forward, a new proposal i s under discussion to create a private guarantee fund to
promote entry o f smaller companies in the insurance market and foster competition. Another
proposal aims at creating an extensive database on the Brazilian insurance market similar to the
SCR for the banking sector. Further steps are being made towards the harmonizationo fbank and
insurance regulation. Brazil looks with interest to international cooperation in the insurance
sector in the framework o f the IAIS. In particular, it i s felt that insurance market cooperation in
Mercosur will mostly benefit smaller member countries.

With the recent rapid growth in the insurance market and the increased diversification and
sophistication o f products, technical capacity within SUSEP i s in clear need o f development.
Some support i s already underway, to help ensure compliance with international standards in
supervisory procedures as well as in valuation and solvency regimes. There is, however,
substantial interest for additional technical assistance to strengthen the technological and
physical infrastructure as well as the organizational and human capabilities necessary for
enhanced on- and off-site supervision o f the insurance sector. This had been identified as a
medium-term action supported by the previous operation and has now become a more urgent
objective to pursue inthe near term.

TheReinsurance Monopoly

To date, there is no competitive re-insurance market in Brazil. Insurance companies have to
apply for reinsurance with the state monopoly Instituto de Reasseguros do Brasil (IRB) and only
if IRB - for lack of capacity or other reasons - authorizes it, then they can turn to foreign
competitors. This has had a detrimental effect on product innovation and risk management
capacity inthe local insurance market inBrazil.

A key law proposal (PLP 249/05) has been presented to Congress this year, regulating a gradual
opening o f the reinsurance sector and reforming IRB role and structure. The law would


  Lei 11,05312004,medidaprovisoria25212005, CNSP Res. 124and 12512005and SUSEP Circ. 293 and 294/2005



                                                            101

categorize re-insurance companies in three segments (local, admitido and eventual) and allow
gradual opening o f the market over time, while still guaranteeing 60% o f the business to local
companies for the first few years. The objective i s to liberalize the market and, at the same time,
avoid unrestricted exposure to international risks throughthe development o f local reinsurance
capacity, It i s expected that a number o f the larger global reinsurance companies would seek to
establish operations in Brazil. Ultimately, it is also possible that some o f them would move
regional operations to Brazil.

Parliamentary approval o f PLP 249 - which had been supported as a medium-term objective in
the previous operation - appears now within reach in the short-term. The expansion o f the
capacity o f the insurance market and the entry o f new service providers will be among the key
outcome indicators to measure the effectiveness o f the new legislation.

The liberalization of the reinsurance sector also calls for a reform o f the role and strategy o f the
state monopoly Instituto de Reasseguros do Brasil within the new competitive environment.
Currently, IRB has both the power to regulate the market and the monopoly over reinsurance
operations. It is owned mostly by the Government, but also by the same insurance companies it
supervises. In the future, the IRB could transfer some o f its statutory tasks o f a supervisory
nature back to the insurance supervisors (CNSP and SUSEP). In recent years, the IRB has been
in an improving financial position though still less than optimally capitalized. Uncertainty and a
lack o f recruitment meant that the staffing resources with relevant expertise have been
progressively eroding. Management at the IRB, together with the Government as the key
shareholder, should take steps to ensure that the company i s well positioned for the future,
consistent with the needs and incentives for both the IRB and the wider insurance sector. Brazil
clearly has the potential to become in the near future the major insurance market in Latin
America as a result o f these trends, transforming its leadership in size into regional leadership in
product innovation and resource mobilization.

Ifwelladministered,theseproductscanprovideusefultoolstobroadenaccesstobasicfinancial

participating institutions' operating costs are covered may make basic accounts more sustainable
inthe medium term than the provision of free services. Untilnow, institutions offering this new

Measures to simplify the procedures for opening bank accounts enhance the efficiency o f
banking and benefit the general investment climate in the country. Therefore, it would be
advisable to gradually extend these types o f initiatives to a considerably wider clientele perhaps
eventually including all bank clients. Many o f these reforms were discussed in the recent Bank
study on access to financial services.'50The increase in the amount o f bank accounts per capita
will be a useful outcome indicator to measure the increase in access to financial services by all
segments o f the population.

Inparticular, payroll deduction loans have been very successful at offering affordable access to
credit for employees. The average monthly rate on these instruments was 2.8 % in November
2004, much lower than the average monthly rate on other personal loans (4.7%). From January to
December o f 2004, the volume o f payroll deduction loans approximately doubled from around
R$ 6 billion to approximately R$ 12 billion. New measures have recently beenpassed allowing
all public sector employees to benefit fiom this product.

Similarly in the insurance sector, a number o f products have been launched in recent years to
expand access to financial services for the poor. In particular, several agricultural insurance
schemes and rural credit mechanisms are now available, including:
1- Private sector agri-business property and casualty insurance (law 10823/2003 and decree
5121/2004);
2- Proagro mais, a Government program within PRONAF providing basic insurance to small
farmers against adverse climate events (CMN resolution no3.234, 31.08.2004);
3- Garantia SAFRA, a shared-premium scheme to create an insurance fund offering basic
coverage for regions particularly subject to drought (eg. the North East).

Additionally, several life and property/casualty insurance policies (seguro popular) have been
designed to offer an affordable basic coverage to underserved segments o f the population. A key
next step supported by this loan is to increase regulatory effort to facilitate market provision o f
basic insurance products for the poor.

Turning to capital markets, some innovative instruments have been proposed in recent years to
channel resources toward "special interest sectors" (such as housing and public infrastructure)

Recently, new measures on credit securitization mechanisms have been approved. Most notably,
Law number 10931/2004 introduced financing and securitization instruments for the real estate
market, including the Property Liens on Real-Estate Developments, the Real Estate Letter o f
Credit and the Real-Estate Credit Bill (LCI). The overall objective o f these measures has been to
reduce the legal uncertainty and the tax burden that private investors are facing in the real estate
market. Additionally, Law 10931/2004 also fully regulates the Bank Credit Bill (LCB), a
securitized credit instrument, backed by existing loans, which ensures expeditious resolution o f

While a new tax law on derivatives transactions has recently been approved (Law 11076/2004), a
liquid local market for derivatives transactions (and in particular futures trading on agricultural
commodities) i s yet to be developed, as the currently few big players prefer to settle their trades
inlarger derivatives exchangesabroad. Furthermore, little progress hasbeen done inthe proposal
of a factoring law, which remains a medium-term objective.

On the other hand, C V M has accomplished a number o f significant reforms related to corporate
governance and in particular protection of minority shareholders. Many listed companies have
now introduced 100% tag-along rights for minority shareholders. Additionally, several
procedural simplifications have significantly reduced the regulatory burden for international
investors.

A key next step is to complete CVM's effort giving a sound legal footing and an efficient
supervisory framework to innovative products which allow wider market access to the
underserved segments o f the population.

Other measures introduced to channel resources to the under-served segments o f the population
are somewhat more controversial as they constitute an increase in Government directed lending
and introduce potentially distortive interest rate ceilings. For example, in force o f regulation
approved in 2003, two percent o f banks' sight deposits are earmarked for microfinance credit

The reservation o f sight deposits for micro-lending at capped interest rates may appear beneficial
in the short-term, but could lead to undesired side-effects in the long run. First, mandatory
microlending increases the danger for banks o f taking on credit risk in excess o f what they would
be able to bear. Banks can protect themselves somewhat against expanded credit risk by
channeling microcredit through other entities, such as SCMs. However, these entities remain
subject to the interest rate ceilings and their capacity to absorb increasing credit risk may be
limited. Second, interest rate ceilings on directed micro-lending can crowd out other micro-
finance lending and might impact adversely on the micro-finance sector, especially, in

combination with the proposed increase in funding costs to TJLP + 8 percent for BNDES

volumes o f funds intended to be made available but also in the interest rate differentials between
these funds, and market rates. Moreover, such low interest rate programs often fail to reach
intendedbeneficiaries.

Admittedly, the requirement to direct two percent o f sight deposits towards micro-loans was
introduced in 2003 in conjunction with an overall reduction o f 15 percent in reserve
requirements. Furthermore, the two percent interest rate cap i s only applied to the smallest size
loans and there i s an expectation that the bulk o f micro-credit would continue at market
determined terms. Thus the Government reckons that Brazil's micro-credit sector overall i s not
being distorted by directed lendingmeasures. Inaddition, at the time o f their introduction, credit
conditions particularly for small businesses were at their tightest in a period o f several years, and
that was also a key rationale for Government action.

In November 2004, the Government launched the National Program o f Oriented Productive
Micro Credit, aimed at expanding access to productive credit for formal and informal
microbusinesses.           This measure effectively increased the interest rate ceiling on microfinance
from 2% to 4% through the provision for a 2% service charge on such transactions. Furthermore,
banks have been allowed to purchase receivables o f MFIs with the objective o f increasing their
indirect participation in microcredit. Finally, the allocation o f FAT funds (Workers' Support
Fund) for microfinance, so far restricted to BNDES, have been made available to other financial
institutions as well, thus increasing competition.

The Oriented Productive Micro-Credit program stresses the importance o f personal contacts and
direct on-site assistance to the borrowing micro-entrepreneurs during the entire duration of the
credit relationship. Inthis sense, it emphasizes knowledge transfer and training o f staff at credit
unions, development agencies, small-business credit companies (SCM) and public-interest civil
society organizations (OSCIP). Additionally, in order to allow access to the resources o f these
financial institutions under safe and affordable conditions, the OSCIP and SCM are supported by
the Endorsement Fund for Micro and Small Companies (FAMPE) - SEBRAE's Endorsement
Fund - as well as by the Endorsement Fund for the Generation o f Employment and Income
(FUNPROGER).

Looking forward, a recent Project0 de Lei Complementar (pre-empresa) i s being discussed to
reduce fiscal and social security requirements on SMEs (with annual gross earnings up to R$ 36
mil). Furthermore, inorder to expedite resolution o f disputes related to microfinance, a draft law
has beenproposed to allow micro-lending institutions to file to small claim courts. This hadbeen
identified among the next steps supported by the previous operation.

Another key next step supported by the previous loan i s a complete evaluation o f new
microfinance measures in terms o f cost and outreach. Progress made so far inthis area i s limited
and a clear indication o f the extent o f the induced distortions i s still missing. It remains an
objective which the current operation expects to be achieved in the medium term. The Ministry


   MedidaProvisoria, n.226 of November 2004



                                                     106

o f Finance is supportingproposals for continued monitoring and assessment o f the net benefits o f
directed lending schemes for microfinance..An important outcome indicator to measure the
increase in access to finance for the poor will be the growth o f non-earmarked credit flowing to
small borrowers and the low-income population.

Another tool to increase access to financial services for the poor in Brazil has been the
development o f a large network o f credit cooperatives. The credit cooperatives sector has grown
in recent ears as membership restrictions have been lifted and more open structures have been
allowed.''         In August 2005, almost 1,400 credit cooperatives were operating in Brazil. The
decision to allow `open admission' credit cooperatives in small municipalities was intended to
stimulate the cooperative sector and help provide financial services in remote areas. However
there may be associated risks. Restrictions o f membership are quite common even in advanced
countries and provide a form o f "reputational collateral." Enhanced supervision i s therefore
needed to contain such risks.

A number o f other measures have recently been passed to allow credit cooperatives to sell a
larger variety o f products, with the objective o f expanding the set o f financial services available
to underserved segments o f the population. At the same time, there has been some progress in
strengthening prudential regulation for credit cooperatives, and measures are being taken at the
Central Bank to include credit cooperatives transactions into the credit registry (SCR) for easier
monitoring. Efforts have also been made to put credit cooperatives and banks on the same
regulatory footing in terms of comparable rules for capital requirements, and to limit the
preferential terms for use o f special Government programs such as the PRONAF. Recent
initiatives passed to harmonize regulatory requirements, strengthen supervision and limit
preferential terms appear key steps in the right direction (Law 10865/04, Res C M N 3198/04,
3309/05, 3321/05 and Circ.BC 3294/05).

               Annex 8: The Judicial System and Its Impact on Contract Enforcernentl6'

For some time, internal and external observers have remarked on the negative impact o f Brazil's
legal and judicial system on private sector development.162 However, the commentaries tend to
be very general, usually mentioning delay, unpredictability, and highcosts, 163in                                       short the nearly
universal set o f complaints about any judiciary, without giving further indication as to the
underlyingcauses or how and where, exactly, these factors impact on private sector transactions.
In fact, for all the criticisms, Brazil's judiciary ranks fairly high as compared to the rest o f the
L A C region, on other key characteristics - independence, professionalism, or even general level
o f public confidence. Brazil i s one o f the first o f the region's judiciaries to have institutionalized
entrance examinations and training programs for judges, and to maintain and use mechanisms for
trackingjudicial output. Moreover, its courts handle and decide on an unusually highcaseload -
figures     provided by the STF, Brazil's Constitutional Court, indicate over 3,000 average annual
filings per judge across the entire system. Obviously, conditions vary widely by region and
across the various state and national judiciaries, but in the federal, labor, and more advanced
state courts, one can hardly accuse the judges o f slacking - not when each member o f the STF,
for example, i s personally responsible for drafting 10,000 opinions annually, a probable world
record, but one also surpassed by the federal social security judges who can issue that many
judgments in a few months.

Giventhis contrast, one could conclude that the complaints are only the "normal" dissatisfaction
with a service that will never meet user expectations, but which objectively may function
relatively well. Be that as it may, they were sufficient to provoke an inter-power battle in 2004
over the Executive's successful promotion o f a series o f constitutional amendments (packaged as
Constitutional Amendment #45) intended to reform the judiciary. One unanticipated benefit of
that battle was an effort by both parties to document their positions, and a resulting expansion o f
the empirically based analysis of the situation. When combined with work done by others
(including the Bank164)this gives a more accurate picture o f the nature and source o f the
complaints, especially, but not exclusively as they relate to contract enforcement and private
sector development. This revisedview can be summarized inthe following five points.

First, while Brazil has a reasonable number of judges (7.7 per 100,000 population, above the
recommended UN average) and only a moderately high litigation rate (10,000 filings per
 100,000 population) the workload is unevenly distributed, with highlevels o f congestion among
courts in the more developed urban areas, and an unusually high level o f filings in second and
third instance courts. Moreover, although judges decide a high number o f cases, they have not
kept up with the exponential growth in demand over the past decade, meaning that they are
sittingon top o f a sizable backlog. Unfortunately, the statistics available do not provide further




``I  Preparedby LinnHammergren. Figures have to be checked as those providedby the MOJ and the STF vary considerably. However, by either
count the figures are high.

l h 2Cite Bank references, others

     While corruption and political or personal pressuresonjudges are sometimes mentioned, Brazil's judiciary has a cleaner reputation than most
inthe region. Infact, the more usual criticism is an excessof independence,not a submission to other powers or political forces.

IM   The World Bank (2004,2005).



                                                                      108

information as to what gets decided at all, within a reasonable time, or i s retired to the backlog
for years165.

Second, whatever the delays in reaching a first instancejudgment, the larger problem i s the high
appeals rate, meaning that for many cases, an initial judgment i s not a final resolution, because
they may well be appealed "all the way to the Supreme Court," or rather both Supreme Courts,
the one corresponding to the jurisdiction (STJ for federal laws, TST for labor, etc) and then the
Constitutional Court (STF).

Third, until a recently (October, 2005) approved law, interlocutory appeals (those regarding
decisions prior to judgment) could be made separately and would stop the forward progess o f a
case until they were decided. The law now makes this practice an exception (only in situations
threatening irreversible damage to the appellant) and instead postpones consideration o f these
issues until the post-judgment phase. It i s anticipated that this will not only eliminate delays
caused by these appeals, but also will discourage their use, as the time-buying effect will not
longer exist.

Fourth, whatever the time to reach a final judgment, collecting awards i s often a lengthy and
ineffectual process.              The process o f enforcement (whether for judicial or extra-judicial
obligations, the latter referring to contracts establishing a right to demand payment, called
executive titles) was fraught with judicial and extra judicial obstacles for the creditor. When
studies like the Bank's "Doing Business" refer to delays and superfluous steps in the process,
they are generally taking this "post-judgment" phase into account, which can take longer than the
time to judgment, if it ever in fact produces any results. Even here, the "judicial" obstacles .are
most usually procedural requirements, not problems originating with the judge. One example i s
the requirement that seized assets be sold in a judicial auction, rather than directly appropriated
(adjudicaqgo) by the plaintiff.

Fifth, court congestion, at all levels, but even more disproportionately in the appeals courts, is
due in substantial part to cases where the Government i s a defendant.                                              Many o f these case
originate in poor service by one o f a few administrative agencies (most notoriously the INSS)
which the plaintiff eventually wins, but only after several years and a series o f appeals by
Government lawyers (who in fact are under legal orders to appeal all but the smallest awards).
A recent study by the Rio de Janeiro state courts166indicates that banks and private utilities also
indulge in this practice because, like the Government, they can "control their caseflow" by thus
delaying payments, and also counting on a certain number o f legitimate claims never going
forward. The courts have introduced a series o f innovations (completely automated processes,
template judgments, court annexed mediation,167etc) to handle this raft o f small, repetitive cases,
which also, i t should be noted, account for a good portion o f the high average caseloads. If

                                                                       109

Brazilianjudges can produce a world record number o f annualjudgments, it i s inpart because so
many o f their cases are o f this type - disputes which arguably should never have to be seen by
the courts.

Thus, while Brazilians' complaints about judicial performance are the standard ones, they
originate in a fairly unusual situation - courts overburdened with a mass o f repetitive cases,
many arising in the failure o f administrative agencies to recognize legitimate claims, an
enormous opportunity for parties to create delays through multiple appeals and the willingness o f
many o f them to use them, and, something more common to the region, the problems associated
with the enforcement o f judgments or of extra-judicial titles.                                           The 2004 Constitutional
Amendment offered little to change this situation with the possible exception of the szimula
vinculante, a measure allowing the STF to declare its constitutional interpretations binding both
on all other judges and on the administrators.

If the szimula works as it is supposed to, it could have an enormous effect in cutting court
congestion (because not only would the repetitive cases be decided in first instance, they would
never get to the court in the first place - as the administrators would also be bound by the
decision) and on reducing delays by limiting appeals. However, there are two cautions here.
These very effects may make the STF cautious about applying the sumula,I6* and any effort to do
so may create its own influx o f appeals, arguing that the cases in question are somehow different
and thus should be excluded from its impacts. Otherwise the CE, while irritating to thejudges in
some o f its details,'69 largely focused on other matters, some o f which may create their own
organizational and procedural problems - the creation o f a new agrarian court system, the
expansion o f the jurisdiction o f the labor courts, or the autonomy given to public defense. None
o f these are necessarily negative changes, but it i s clear no one has thought out how they will be
implemented, with what funding, and with what consequences for how conflicts are resolved.
However, any resulting problems are for the future, and for the meantime, the question i s how to
resolve the existing ones.

The legal experts backing the reforms, including the President o f the STF, the Minister o f
Justice, the head o f the Ministry's Judicial Reform Division, and various private attorneys,
intended to attack the delay and congestion problems in a less dramatic fashion after the
Constitutional Amendment had been approved. They proposed to do this through a series o f
targeted amendments to the civil procedures and other basic codes, and by fomenting certain
administrative improvements within the courts (where the role o f the National Council could be
key, assuming it can rise to the occasion). Thus within months o f the CE 45's passage, the
Ministry o f Justice sent 26 reform bills to the Congress, all intended to eliminate the bottlenecks

its experts had identified in the civil, labor, and criminal proceedings. As o f October, 2005, one
o f these had become law - that affecting the most common interlocutory appeals, and requiring
that they be handled during the ordinary post-judgment appeal period. Other proposed changes
to the civil code involve the following:


     0  P L 52/04: Changes to the enforcement o f judgments, making this part o f the initial
        proceeding (and not a separate process), thereby eliminating the bottleneck encountered
        in (re)notifying the defendant, and introducing a 10 percent fine for nonpayment within
        15 days o f the judgment; giving the creditor the right to select assets for seizure; and
        greatly reducing the defendant's ability to suspend enforcement by offering new protests.
     0  PL 4497/04: Making similar changes to proceedings involving the enforcement o f extra-
        judicial titles. Also allowing the claimant to seize the embargoed asset directly, rather
        than having to depend on a lengthy, and often ineffectualjudicial auction
     0  PL 90/2005: (Revises a proposal by the Congress.) Introduces the szimula impeditiva de
        recursos , similar to the szimula vinculante, except that this refers to interpretations o f
        infraconstitutional law made by the STJ (highest federal court) and may be used by
        judges as abasis for their own decisions and to deny appeals contrary to the STJ rulings.
     0  P L 4728/04: Allows a judge reviewing a series o f repetitive cases one o f which he has
        already dismissed for lack o fmerit, to do the same for the others.
     0  P L 4723/04 - Unifies jurisprudence for Turmas Recursais, used to handle appeals from
        small claims courts.

Beyond this, the Ministry Secretaria de Reforma do Judiciario, in conjunction with other
reformers and with Fazenda's Secretaria de Politica Econdmica, i s looking into additional legal
and structural changes, including the interconnection and standardization o f the property and
other registries, currently run by independent notaries (carturios) and the promotion o f "best
practices" within courts aimed at increasing efficiency. The Secretaria, with outside financing
including that o f the Bank, is conducting a series o f studies to support its proposals and to
investigate additional areas for improvement. At the same time the STF and the Council, as well
as some other high courts (STF, TST, and TJs o f state systems) are working to improve their
statistical systems for monitoring overall performance, promoting exchanges o f innovative
practices, and doing some o f their own analytic work. Finally, given Government concerns
about the judicial debt, its impact on court performance, the courts' unanticipatedability to move
these cases faster, and the effects of mechanisms like the szimula vinculante, both Fazenda and
Justiga, as well as the Advogado Geral da UniZo have begun to study the issues more carefully -
to determine what the actual and potential liabilities are and review alternatives for dealing with
them, as well as for preventing further proliferation.

The general belief is that no single change will alter the system significantly, and thus that this
collection o f measures will all be required. Inthe end, what i s needed i s a cultural change to end
a series o f interrelated practices with deep structural and behavioral roots, and from which most
of the major players derive some benefit. Judges do not benefit from delay, but they also won't
benefit much from ending it, and the congestion itself validates the sums spent on the courts.
There are other actors, however, with stronger stakes in the system -the private bar, the
Government, the banks and utilities - who may be less interested in rapid, or in some cases, any
change. Finally, many o f the changes run against values - the right to a second, third or endless



                                                  111

number o f reviews o f a decision - held even by those not directly affected. This suggests the
further need for studies and discussions to advance an understanding o f the costs o f the current
system, costs not only to entrepreneurs, but to all citizens. Justifying the reforms in terms o f
their impacts on investment i s thus probably not a wise tactic - and in effect, the benefits go
beyond the entrepreneurial class. Eventhe poor need contracts enforced as well as a measure o f
juridical security.

In the meantime, there is also ample room for the introduction of organizational and
technological innovations to help courts move their cases more rapidly. This will not resolve the
entire problem; as the above analysis suggests, the notion that things will be better ifjudges just
work faster i s vastly overrated. It also could further delay attention to the more fundamental
changes required, but as these administrative fixes are popular with the judges and seem
unopposed by the other parties, they are worth the effort, and in some cases ('judiciaries in less
developed states, or the state judiciary o f SBo Paulo, currently in threat o f collapse under its
enormous workload) may make a real difference. Better system and courtroom administration
(the aim o f these quick fixes) has visibly improved performance o f the most advanced courts,
and also i s a means o f identifying and isolating the more profound structural causes o f user
dissatisfaction.

The loan's judicial section will thus focus on three dimensions o f change:


     0   Efforts to reduce delays causedby excessive appeals and congested dockets
     0   Efforts to improve the enforcement o fjudgments, especially incivil cases
     a   Efforts to increase court efficiency and expand access through ICT, administrative
        reorganizations, and the addition o f alternative services (e.g. small claims courts,
        mediation)

It i s anticipated that the first and third dimensions will proceed most rapidly, the first because
key legislation, including constitutional reforms, has already been enacted, the third because of
its inherent appeal. The second area i s inherently more problematic because the key bills are still
pending congressional approval in a political environment that does not appear propitious. It is
added because . o f its importance, but advances in the other two may in the end be the
conditionality that counts.




                                                 112

                 Annex 9: A Noteon Growthand ProductivityProspectsin Brazil'70


Understand the disappointing performance o f Brazil GDP growth in the eighties and the
persistence of volatile and low growth rates since the macroeconomic stabilization in the last
decade constitute one o f the most relevant issues in the contemporaneous economic debate. The
objective o f this note i s to address two questions: what are the reasons behind the slump o f the
growth rate from an average o f 9% during the period 1968-1980 to an average growth rate o f
2.2% from 1990 up to now? And even more relevant, there are sufficient reasons to foresee a
sustainable increase o f growth rates in the next years?
With respect to the first question, the fall o f capital accumulation was the underlyingcause o fthe
Brazilian GDP collapse in the eighties. Visibly, the drop o f gross savings, which expressed the
macroeconomic disequilibria o f this period, was the main reason for the deceleration o f capital
accumulation. However, the increase in the price o f capital goods, the fall o f capital productivity
and capacity utilization exacerbated the negative impact o f the decline o f savings on capital
accumulation.
Total factor productivity has also played an important role on GDP growth performance. In the
high-growth period (late sixties to early seventies), total factor productivity reinforced the
positive impact o f the stepping up o f capital accumulation. In the last ten years, total factor
productivity growth was the major force behindthe smooth increase o f GDP growth rates.
With respect to the second question, the external and fiscal adjustments experienced by the
Brazilian economy since 1999 and accelerated since 2003, have created more favorable
conditions that permit to be optimistic with relation to the growth prospects in the next years,
The impressive increase of gross savings o f the last six years should foster the acceleration of
capital accumulation. Additionally, the maintenance o f the favorable evolution o f capacity
utilization, o f total factor productivity and o f employment would strengthen the effect of
increasing savings on capital accumulation. The strengthening o f the trade liberalization and the
reversal o f the increasing trend of the price o f capital goods also should favor capital
accumulation and growth inthe next years.
This note is divided in two parts. In answering the first question above, the next section is
dedicated to analyze the long run determinants o f Brazil GDP growth. The second scenario,
conjectures on the fulfillment of necessary conditions to increase growth rates in a sustainable
manner.

Longruntrendsfor growth,capitalaccumulationandproductivity
Since 1960, the Brazilian growth performance can be divided intwo periods: from 1960 to 1980,
characterized by a strong growth performance period with annual growth rates averaging 8% and
from 1981 to 2005, period of weak growth performance with average annual growth rates of
2.2%.
However, a more accurate examination suggests that behind the distinction above based on
growth rate performances, there were different growth patterns within each period. Inparticular,



"" Preparedby Fernando Blanco.



                                                   113

investigating the role played by capital accumulation and total factor productivity, it is possible
to identify five periods since the sixties (Bonelli, 2005):17'
          from 1960 to 1967, the early military years when capital accumulation was the major
          factor behind GDP growth with total factor productivity growth played a minor role;

          from 1967 to 1973, period called as the Brazilian economic miracle, despite that capital
          accumulation continued to be the largest contributor o f growth, total factor productivity
          contribution increased substantially fostering the growth acceleration;
          from 1974 to 1980, the late seventies period, growth was driven by a strong capital
          accumulation while the contribution o f total factor productivity fell substantially having a
          negative contribution to growth;
          from 1981 to 1992, period called as the lost decade, the fall on capital accumulation was
          the driven force behind the slowdown o f the Brazilian economy, however, the strong and
          negative contribution o f total factor productivity further worsened the growth
          performance o f the Brazilian economy, and,

           finally, from 1993 to 2004, a period o f macroeconomic stabilization that began with the
           launch o f the Real stabilization plan i s characterized by the continuity of the slow capital
           accumulation o f the previous period and with total factor productivity being the main
           driving force o fthe economic recovery.

recognize the important role played by total factor productivity inspecific periods.
The strong capital accumulation was the main force behind the period o f high growth rates that
characterized the sixties and seventies. On the opposite way, the fall o f capital accumulation was

the lost decade (1981-1992) and contributed decisively to the smooth growth recovery o f the

The role o f total factor productivity can be better evaluated trough the analysis o f the changes
between periods. Total factor productivity plays a relevant role in explaining the differences in
GDP growth rates between the different periods. In fact, total factor productivity had a
contribution almost similar than the contribution o f capital accumulation (38% vis a vis 41%) to
the growth acceleration o f the economic miracle, when the average growth rate rose by 6.1
percentage points. The deceleration o f the late seventies also can be explained mostly by the
deceleration o f total factor productivity growth (contribution of 61%). The fall o f total factor
productivity also was relevant for economic downturn o f the lost decade expressed in a growth
rate 5.5% lower than the observed in the previous period. Finally, total factor productivity
recovery was the exclusive driver (contribution of 112%) o f the smooth upturn observed since
the Real Plan.

As mentioned above, growth trends in Brazil were defined by the rhythm of capital
accumulation. In turn, as the evolution o f capital accumulation depends primarily on the ability
to generate savings, it i s necessary to analyze the,evolutiono f gross savings.
Savings was the most important determinant o f capital accumulation until the eighties. It rose
substantially from 14% o f GDP in 1960 to 18% o f GDP fostering the acceleration o f capital
accumulation which explains the impressive GDP growth rates during the Brazilian economic
miracle, Highdomestic saving rate and increasing external indebtedness were responsible for the
continuation o f the capital accumulation which maintained high economic growth rates even
with the decline o f total factor productivity. In the eighties the savings rate fell to 20% o f GDP
and strongly fluctuated around the interval between 18%-20% henceforth.
Despite the coincidence between the increase o f savings and capital accumulationup to 1980 and
the decline o f both during the eighties, the fall o f savings is not sufficient to explain the extent o f
the drop o f capital accumulation. Also, the highvolatility exhibited by the savings rates was not
corresponded by a similar movement o f the capital accumulation which presented a continuous
declining tendency in the eighties and nineties. Thus, additional explanatory factors explain the
slump of capital accumulation.

 Expressed in this way and analyzing the evolution o f the variables above, it i s possible to
 perceive that negative impact o f the fall o f gross savings on capital accumulation was
 strengthened by the by the increase o f relative price o f capital goods, by the fall o f productivity
 of capital and by the drop o f capacity utilization.
 First, the increase o f the relative price of capital goods reduced the purchasingpower of savings.
 As Figure 3, shows the price o f capital goods compared with the GDP deflator increases
 continuously from 1970 to 1990, deepening the negative impact o f the fall o f savings in capital
 accumulation and explaining why the fall o f capital accumulation was more pronounced than the
 fall o f savings. The increase o f relative price o f capital goods i s associated to the import
 substitution strategy adopted by Brazil since the fifties and reinforced in the seventies, that
 increased the market power of the capital good domestic producers and generated allocative
 inefficiencies that affected the overall productivity o f the economy.175

 Second, the fall o f the output to capital ratio which measure the productivity o f capital, was
 another factor explaining the slowdown o f capital accumulation. Figure 2 shows that the
 productivity fell strongly since 1973 until 1984 fluctuating around a decreasing path until 1992.
 Since 1992 to 2004 capital productivity stabilized at its lowest levels inthe last fifty years.

 It is important to mention that the fall o f the output to capital ratio was not exclusive o f the
 Brazilian economy and that the decreasing path o f capital productivity was a worldwide trend
 that also initiated in the early seventies with the first oil shock. However the Brazilian capital
 productivity was always lower than the world average176.Again, the inefficiencies generated by
 the import substitutionstrategy can explain the deeper fall o f Brazilian capital productivity.
 Third, the fall o fthe capacity utilization is other factor that explains the slower rhythmo f capital
 accumulation. Figure 4 shows that besides the normal cycles, after a strong decreasing trend
 since the late seventies to the mid eighties the Brazilian economy did not achieve the levels o f
 capacity utilization observed inthe seventies.

As mentioned above, the contribution o f total factor productivity varied significantly throughout
the period o f analysis having a pro-cyclical pattern: total factor productivity growth was a
relevant factor explaining the growth acceleration o f the period 1967-73 while its long fall in the
eighties accompanied the strong GDP slowdown that characterized the lost decade. Finally, the
total factor productivity recovery o f the period 1993-2004 was the only driving force o f the
smooth recovery o f GDP in a context o f low capital accumulation that persisted in the last
decade177.Without the recovery o f total factor productivity, GDP growth rate would be very
similar to the observed in the eighties.

capital accumulationfinancedby high domestic savingsand indebtedness.

  Finally, labor force supply was not an impediment for economic growth inBrazil. Until 1980,
the contribution of raw labor to growth was about 20% and had a similar contribution to the
change o f growth rates between periods. Given the strong fall o f total factor productivity, its
contribution to growth in the lost decade increased to 83%, but its contribution to the fall of GDP
growth inthis period was low (6%). More interestingi s the negative contribution o f labor during
the recovery observed inthe Real period which expresses the low employment absorption that
characterized the stabilization period.
More relevant than the quantitative dimension o f labor supply, two qualitative aspects need to be
considered: the quality o f the labor force and the quality o f the jobs. Pessoa et a1 (2003), found a
reduced contribution o f human capital (20%) to growth in the last fifty years. However, the
importance o f human capital dramatically increased (to 47%) in the nineties.' 78 Coincidently,
Brazil experienced a continuous but slow improvement o f educational indicators since the
seventies that were accelerated inthe nineties.
The worsening of jobs quality has weakened the contribution o f labor .to growth. In particular,
the growth o f the informal sector where productivity i s much lower than in the formal sector
affected negatively overall productivity. The negative contribution o f employment to the
resumption o f growth in the Real period reflects the fact that formal employment has been
growing at lower rates than the labor force.
Insummary, the deceleration of capital accumulationwas behind the slowdown of the Brazilian
economy in the eighties. The low capital accumulation was the result o f the fall o f savings,
capital productivity and capacity utilization and o f the increase o f capital goods prices. On the
other side, total factor productivity strengthened the acceleration o f capital accumulation during
the Brazilian economic miracle period and was the driven force o f the economic recovery in the
Real era.

Recent trends and prospects for the next years
Despite the recovery o f economic growth o f the macroeconomic stabilization years, the growth
performance o f Brazilian economy in the last ten years has not been satisfactory. Also, the
current values for the variables that define the growth prospects indicate an equilibrium growth
rate for the Brazilian economy varying between 2.5% and 3% which are lower than the observed
in other emergent countries and clearly much lower than the experienced by Brazil during the
period 1960-198.
 It is possible to increase the growth rate o f the Brazilian economy and make it sustainable? The
recent developments occurred since 1999 and especially since 2003 provide solid arguments to
 sustain that Brazil can grow faster and ina sustainable way.
The strong external and fiscal adjustments experienced by the Brazilian economy since 1999 and
 strengthened since 2003 have created positive macroeconomic conditions for the increase of
acceleration o f economic growth in the next years. Inparticular, the recent evolution o f savings,
total factor productivity, capacity utilization and formal employment depict a more favorable
growth scenario.
 First, the external adjustment, expressed in the impressive turnaround o f the current account
balance from -4.7% o f GDP in 1999 to a positive 2% o f GDP in 2004/05, has implied the
 increase o f gross domestic savings from 15% in 1999 domestic to 23% in 2004/05. Differently
 from the external adjustment o f the early eighties which was based on the strong reduction o f
 capital accumulation, the current external adjustment effort has been based on the reduction o f
 consumption which has fallen from 81% o f GDP in 1999 to 74% o f GDP in 2004.179
The maintenance o f the current level o f domestic savings and a likely future reduction o f the
 current account balance (which means an increase o f external savings) would finance the
 increase o f the investment from the current 20% o f GDP to 25% o f GDP and would improve the
 growth prospects o f the Brazilian economy. In a scenario where the relative price o f capital
 goods, the productivity o f capital and the capacity utilization remain the same, the solely increase
 of savings would allow the acceleration o f capital accumulation that would promote the rise o f
 the growth rate o f the Brazilian economy from the current 2.5% to 4%.
The recent evolution of total factor productivity also indicates an improvement in Brazil's
 growth potential. The average total factor productivity growth since 2000 was 0.5%. In 2004,
 given its pro-cyclical nature, it jumped to 1.5% value which i s similar to the growth rate o f
productivity o f USA. Assuming a PTF average growth o f 1.5% for the next years, the potential
 growth rate o f the Brazilian economy would be similar to the obtained in 2004 which was 5%.
The current performance o f employment also permits to envisage a more favorable growth
prospects. In particular, the strong increase o f formal employment in the last three years i s
 strengthening the contribution o f labor to growth. At this respect it i s important to note, that
differently from the nineties, since 2002, the increase o f total factor productivity was
 accompanied by the increase of formal employment. Figure 5 below shows that labor
productivity increased by 15% since June 2003 and this increase was accompanied by an
increase o f 6% in industrial employment. The increase o f productivity in the last two years

contrasts with the increase experienced in the nineties when the positive performance o f
productivity was inpart the result o f the reduction o f employment.
In the same direction, the strong improvements in educational indicators observed in the last
years also suggest a better growth scenario for the next years. At this respect, Paes de Barros et a1
(2004), found that the increase o f labor productivity resulted from the improvement o f labor
qualification which explains 87% o f the productivity growth while the quality o f jobs explains

The evolution o f the capacity utilization since 2004 also suggests an optimistic expectation for
the fbture. In the previous section, it was pointed out that since the seventies the Brazilian
economy was being unable to reach the capacity utilization levels observed in the period o f the
economic miracle. However, Figure 3 above shows that in2004 and 2005 has reached very high
capacity utilization levels (the highest since 1977). This increase o f capacity utilization should
favor the acceleration o f capital accumulation.
The recent evolution o f the price o f capital goods does not depict an optimistic view on the
impact o f this variable on capital accumulation. As Figure 2 showed, the relative price o f capital
goods increased continuously since 1999 without any reversal movement. However,
disaggregating the price o f capital goods in machinery and civil construction, it i s possible to
note that the increasing trend o f the relative price of capital goods can be explainedby the higher
increase of civil construction prices. Only in 1999 with the strong exchange rate devaluation, the
relative price o f machinery and equipment experienced a partial recovery.lgl                                         At this respect, it
i s reasonable to expect that the exchange rate appreciation o f the last two years and Government
initiatives towards the reduction o f the taxation o f machinery and equipments will induce the fall
o f the price o f machinery and equipment.
Related to the relative weight o f civil construction vis a vis machinery and equipment, inthe last
years it was observed a change in the composition o f investments. In particular, the fall o f civil
construction explained by the reduction o f public investment in infrastructure reduced the share
o f civil construction on total capital formation from 82 percent in 1999 to 76 percent in 2004.

In summary, recent developments on the growth explanatory variables define more favorable
prospects for growth. On the macroeconomic side, the consolidation of stabilization since 1999
and strengthened in2003, resulted in the impressiveincrease o f savings, has generated necessary
conditions for a higher and more sustainable capital accumulation and growth performance. The
increase o f productivity, capacity utilization and employment also should induce the acceleration
of capital accumulation and growth rates.

                    Annex 10: International Monetary FundRelations Note


On December 7, 2005, the Executive Board o f the InternationalMonetary Fund(IMF) concluded
Post-Program Monitoring discussions with Brazil based on the information available through
that date.

Background- Notwithstanding the difficult political context since May 2005, macroeconomic
performance has remained robust, underpinned by strong macroeconomic policies and favorable
external conditions. After slowing in the fourth quarter o f 2004 and the first quarter o f 2005,
economic activity rebounded strongly in the second quarter, before declining markedly in the
third quarter o f 2005, mainly reflecting inventory decumulation. As a result, real GDP grew by
an average o f 2% percent (year-on-year) during the first three quarters o f 2005. Growth has
remained supported by private consumption-reflecting           steady gains in real incomes and
employment, and rapid growth in household credit-and        by net exports.

The strong fiscal performance o f recent years has continued, with the consolidated primary fiscal
surplus above the 2005 target. This improvement reflects continued strong tax collections-
particularly income taxes-as     well as higher primary surpluses o f subnational Governments and
public sector enterprises. However, pension imbalances have continued to grow. The prudent
tightening of monetary policy initiated in September 2004 and the appreciation o f the exchange
rate helped bring the 12-month headline inflation down from a peak o f 8 percent in May to 6.4
percent in October. With inflation pressures abating and inflation expectations virtually
converging to the end-2005-06 targets, the central bank has lowered its policy interest rate since
September, by a total o f 125 basis points to 18% percent.

The balance o f payments position has remained strong. Export performance has continued to
exceed expectations, supported by rapid global growth and high commodity prices,
notwithstanding the appreciation o f the real in real effective terms since early 2005.
Private capital inflows have also strengthened, driven by higher foreign direct investment and
renewed portfolio net inflows. Brazilian corporates have continued to deleverage and external
debt has declined further.

Public sector net debt has declined further and its structure has improved further. Several
important steps were taken to improve the maturity profile and extend the duration o f public
debt, including the issuance for the first time o f a US$1.5 billion 10-year real-denominated
external sovereign bond in September. Nevertheless, the share o f floating-rate domestic debt
remains high and, while improving at the margin, the average duration o f public debt i s still
short. The central bank resumed intervention in the foreign exchange market in early October
aimed at further buildingup international reserves, which have continued to rise relative to short-
term external debt.

Against this backdrop, financial market sentiment has remained favorable, albeit with bouts o f
volatility. The real attained its highest level since early 2001, sovereign bond spreads narrowed
to all time lows, and stock prices reached historical highs. Since mid-October, Moody's upgraded




                                                  124

Brazil's sovereign credit rating in line with other credit rating agencies, while other credit rating
agencies have upgraded the outlook from stable to positive.

The strong macroeconomic performance, together with the increased coverage o f the Bolsa
Familia program, has led to a significant reduction in poverty and income inequality between
2002 and 2004. The poverty rate has declined to its lowest level since 1992, with the share o f
people below the poverty line declining to 25.1 percent in2004, from 27.3 percent in 2003, well
below the 35.9 percent rate in 1992. The strengthening o f the Bolsa Familia program, along with
higher employment in the formal labor market, allowed for a significant improvement in the
income o f the poorest groups and for a reduction inthe income gap between formal and informal
workers for the first time in the decade. Income inequality has also declined to its lowest level
since 1981.

Executive Board Assessment

Executive Directors commended the Brazilian authorities for the impressive performance o f the
economy since the end o f the stand-by arrangement. Directors attributed this performance to the
authorities' commitment to sustain and strengthen the macroeconomic policy framework and to
the comprehensive reforms implemented over the past several years, together with supportive
external conditions. The combination o f highpublic sector primary surpluses, inflation targeting,
and a flexible exchange rate regimehas proven highly successful inreducing vulnerabilities and
keeping financial market confidence high, despite uncertain political conditions. Directors also
commended the authorities for the significant progress made inreducing poverty inrecent years,
thus enabling Brazil to continue lowering income inequality and to make progress toward
achieving the MillenniumDevelopment Goals.

Directors viewed the Brazilian economy as well placed for continued favorable economic
performance. Following buoyant economic growth in2004, the economy has expanded at a more
sustainable pace in 2005. Export performance has remained strong, notwithstanding the marked
appreciation o f the real, while on the domestic side rising employment and real incomes have
boosted consumer expenditures. Directors noted the unfavorable outcome for GDP in the third
quarter, but observed that this seemed to reflect mainly inventory adjustment and other specific
factors, and that the underlying trend o f domestic demand remains positive.

With balance sheet vulnerabilities still a concern and the risks posed by the external and
domestic environment, Directors welcomed the authorities' commitment to sustain strong
policies. Public debt remains high and o f relatively short maturity, with a large share still linked
to short-term interest rates. Along with sizable external borrowing needs, these factors still leave
the Brazilian economy exposed to abrupt changes in global conditions and the domestic political
scene.

Directors commended the prudent conduct o f monetary policy in the context o f inflation
targeting and a flexible exchange rate policy. Inflation i s expected to continue to decelerate,
reflecting the convergence o f expectations to the official end-2006 target and the modest
adjustment in administered prices expected for 2006. Directors considered that the benign
inflation outlook provides significant scope for further steady easing o f the monetary policy
stance, but maintaining the cautious approach adopted by the Central Bank-given          the need to


                                                125

consolidate the gains against inflation, the uncertainties surrounding the transmission mechanism
of monetary policy, and the risks in the environment. Inthis context, Directors noted the reforms
to the credit market and the improvements in central bank liquidity management that have
strengthened monetary policy, and encouraged the authorities to continue with these reforms. A
number o f Directors also suggested that the present favorable macroeconomic conditions provide
a good opportunity to simplify further and reduce reserve requirements, and thus help reduce the
highcosts o f financial intermediation. Directors welcomed the staffs conclusion that the banking
sector remains sound; nevertheless, they advised keeping under close monitoring the rapid
growth incredit.

Directors commended the authorities for the prudent conduct o f fiscal policy in 2005, and
encouraged the authorities to preserve the strong stance o f fiscal policy inthe period ahead. Most
Directors agreed that the primary fiscal balance should remain the key policy target, and stressed
the importance o f maintaining the strong observed performance with regard to the consolidated
primary surplus, in order to maintain the progress toward debt reduction, provide full scope for
monetary policy easing, and protect economic performance from unexpected volatility in2006.

Directors stressed the importance o f lowering the tax burden, simplifying the tax system,
and reducing spending rigidities while continuing to lower public indebtedness. In this regard,
Directors encouraged the Government to develop an agenda for comprehensive fiscal reforms.
They noted that the new ceilings on expenditure and revenue, introduced in the 2006 budget
guidelines law, signal the authorities' desire to contain the steady rise in the tax burden and
public spending, while helping to shift expenditure priorities to needed infrastructure and to build
a consolidated fiscal structure for the future. At the same time, some Directors observed that the
ceilings could imply greater complexity in budgetary management. A number o f Directors also
viewed the introduction o f a countercyclical adjustment in the 2006 budget guidelines law as
premature. A few Directors, however, saw the mechanism for countercyclical adjustments more
favorably, if implemented with needed safeguards. Directors stressed that a key element in a
package o f fiscal reforms would be to increase budget flexibility, while reducingtax distortions.

Directors commended the authorities' prudent debt management and the buildup o f international
reserves, which have contributed to reducing vulnerabilities. They welcomed the progress made
inprefinancing sovereign external needs, improvingthe structure o f public debt, and developing
domestic capital markets-including     with the recent issuance o f the real-denominated sovereign
bond and the substantial overall reduction in the public sector's exposure to currency risk. With
reduced macroeconomic volatility and a downward sloping yield curve, Directors felt that
conditions are favorable for a significant improvement in the structure o f public debt, and
particularly for further gains in overall debt maturity. Directors encouraged the authorities to
continue their efforts to develop domestic capital markets by further facilitating access to foreign
investors and by phasing out taxes on financial transactions. They also welcomed the authorities'
commitment to continue building up international reserves, while preservinga flexible exchange
rate regime.

Directors commended the authorities for the progress made with their poverty reduction agenda.
They welcomed the evidence that sustained growth, lower inflation, higher formal employment,
and the expansion o f social programs have led to a significant improvement in social conditions



                                                126

and living standards. Directors commended the reduction in the poverty rate and in income
inequality in2004 to their lowest levels since 1992 and 1981, respectively.

Directors urged the authorities to reinvigorate the reform agenda to boost medium-term growth
and to help further reduce poverty and inequality. They regretted that the political situation in
2005 has delayed the Government's reform agenda in congress, while they foresee further
limitations to progress in the run-up to the general elections in 2006. In this regard, they
encouraged the authorities to make efforts to advance key reform priorities, including with
respect to the harmonization o f the state value added tax system; the establishment o f formal
central bank autonomy; the implementation o f regulations for complementary pension funds for
civil servants; and steps for further improvement in the business climate.
